Compound Syllogisms: Structure and Omission in Rhetoric
Author explains how compound syllogisms omit obvious steps in rhetorical speech, with hadith example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the omitted intermediate conclusions in a compound syllogism and reconstruct the full syllogistic chain from a given rhetorical passage.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism hides its full logic by dropping obvious links, yet remains valid.

In natural speech, especially in teaching and preaching, we often skip steps that are too clear to need stating. This lesson unpacks one such example from classical logic: a multi-step proof that the world is originated. You will learn to recognize the hidden syllogisms and restore the missing conclusions, and then apply the same skill to a prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the final conclusion
Find the ultimate claim the passage is trying to prove. Here it is 'the world is originated'.
2
List the explicit premises
The passage gives a chain: every body is composed; every composed thing has an inseparable accident; every accident is originated; etc. Write them down in order.
3
Reconstruct the intermediate conclusions
Between each pair of premises, a logical step is omitted. For example, from 'every body is composed' and 'every composed thing has an inseparable accident', the omitted conclusion is 'every body has an inseparable accident'. Do this for each link.
The author explains that each omitted conclusion is clear and thus not stated.
4
Check the logical flow
Verify that each intermediate conclusion follows from the preceding premises and serves as a premise for the next step. The chain should be valid.
5
Apply the same method to the hadith
The hadith gives two premises: 'a person dies upon what he lived' and 'is resurrected upon what he died'. Identify the terms (life, death, resurrection) and deduce the omitted conclusion.
The author identifies life as minor term, death as middle, resurrection as major.

والمخاطبات التعليمية. ومن جملة التركيبات ما تترك فيه النتائج الواضحة وبعض المقدمات، ويذكر من كلّ قياس مقدمة واحدة وتترتب بعضها على بعض وتساق إلى نتيجة واحدة كقولنا : كلّ جسم مؤلّف، وكلّ مؤلّف فمقارن لعرض لا ينفك عنه، وكلّ عرض فحادث، وكلّ مقارن لحادث لا يتقدم عليه وكلّ ما لا يتقدم عليه فوجوده معه وكلّ ما وجوده معه فهو حادث، فإذن العالم حادث. وكلّ واحدة من هذه المقدمات تمامها بقياس كامل حذفت نتائجها وما ظهر من مقدماتها وسيقت لغرض واحد وإلا فكان ينبغي أن يقول : كل جسم مؤلف، وكلّ مؤلف فمقارن لعرض لا ينفك عنه، فإذن كلّ جسم فمقارن لعرض لا ينفك عنه. ثم يبتدي ويضيف إليه مقدمة أخرى، وهو أن كلّ مقارن لعرض لا ينفك عنه. فإذن كلّ جسم فمقارن لعرض لا ينفك عنه، ثم يبتدي ويضيف إليه مقدمة أن كلّ مقارن لعرض لا ينفك عنه فهو مقارن لحادث، ثم يشتغل بما بعده على الترتيب، ولكن أغنى وضوح هذه النتائج عن التصريح بها. وربما تجري في المخاطبات كلمات لها نتائج لكن تترك تلك النتائج، إما لظهورها وإما لأنها لا تقصد للاحتجاج، بل تذكر المقدمات تعريفاً لها في أنفسها اعتماداً على قبول المخاطب، فقد قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : يموت المرء على ما عاش عليه، ويحشر على ما مات عليه. وهاتان مقدمتان نتيجتهما أن المرء يحشر على ما عاش عليه فحالة الحياة هي الحد الأصغر، وحالة الممات الحد الأوسط. ومهما ساوت حالة الحشر حالة الموت، وساوت حالة الموت حالة الحياة، فقد ساوت حالة الحشر حالة الحياة. والمقصود من سياق الكلام تنبيه الخلق على أن الدنيا مزرعة الآخرة، ومنها التزوّد. ومن لم يكتسب السعادة وهو في الدنيا فلا سبيل له إلى اكتسابها بعد موته، فمن كان في هذه أعمى فهو عند الموت أعمى، أعني عمى البصيرة عن درك الحق والعياذ بالله. ومن كان عند الموت أعمى فهو عند الحشر أعمى كذلك، بل هو أضل سبيلا إذ ما دام الإنسان في الدنيا فله أمل في الطلب، وبعد الموت قد تحقق اليأس. والمقصود أن الكلمات الجارية في المحاورات كلها أقيسة محرفة غيرت تأليفاتها للتسهيل، فلا ينبغي أن يغفل الإنسان عنها بالنظر إلى الصور، بل ينبغي لا يلاحظ إلا الحقائق المعقولة دون الألفاظ المنقولة.
And educational discourses. Among the compositions are those in which the clear results and some premises are left out, and one premise is mentioned from each syllogism, and they are arranged in order and driven to a single conclusion, as we say: Every body is composed, and every composed thing is accompanied by an inseparable accident, and every accident is originated, and everything accompanied by an originated thing does not precede it, and everything that does not precede it has its existence with it, and everything whose existence is with it is originated; therefore the world is originated. And each of these premises is the completion of a complete syllogism whose results and what appeared of its premises were omitted, and they are driven for a single purpose. Otherwise it would be necessary to say: Every body is composed, every composed thing is accompanied by an inseparable accident; therefore every body is accompanied by an inseparable accident. Then he begins and adds another premise, that everything accompanied by an inseparable accident is accompanied by an originated thing, then he engages with what follows in order, but the clarity of these results made explicit mention unnecessary. And sometimes in discourses expressions occur that have results, but those results are left out, either because of their obviousness or because they are not intended for argumentation, rather the premises are mentioned as a definition in themselves, relying on the acceptance of the interlocutor. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'A person dies upon what he lived, and is resurrected upon what he died upon.' These are two premises whose conclusion is that a person is resurrected upon what he lived upon: the state of life is the minor term, and the state of death is the middle term. Whenever the state of resurrection equals the state of death, and the state of death equals the state of life, then the state of resurrection equals the state of life. The purpose of the speech is to alert the creatures that this world is the farm of the hereafter, and from it one prepares provisions. Whoever does not acquire happiness while in this world has no way to acquire it after death. So whoever is blind in this world is blind at death, I mean blindness of insight from perceiving truth, and may Allah protect. And whoever is blind at death is blind at resurrection likewise, indeed he is even more astray in path, because as long as a person is in this world he has hope for seeking, but after death despair has become certain. The point is that the expressions current in conversations are all transformed syllogisms, their compositions changed for ease, so a person should not be heedless of them by looking at the forms, but rather should observe only the intelligible realities, not the transmitted words.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage discusses compound syllogisms in rhetorical discourse. It begins by explaining that in educational conversations, some clear conclusions and premises are omitted for brevity. The author gives an example of a chain of syllogisms proving that the world is originated: every body is composed, every composed thing has an inseparable accident, every accident is originated, etc., leading to the conclusion that the world is originated. He then unpacks the full form, showing that each step could be a complete syllogism, but the intermediate conclusions are omitted because they are obvious. He emphasizes that the removal of intermediate conclusions is acceptable due to their clarity. Then he gives a hadith example: 'A person dies upon what he lived, and is resurrected upon what he died.' He analyzes this as a syllogism: the state of life is the minor term, the state of death is the middle term, and the state of resurrection is the major term. The conclusion is that the resurrection state equals the life state, because they both equal the death state. The author then explains the moral purpose: to remind that this world is the farm of the hereafter, and one must acquire happiness here. He concludes that all ordinary speech contains modified syllogisms, and one should focus on the logical meanings, not the literal words. This guards against the misunderstanding that the omitted steps indicate a lack of logical structure.

الحد الأوسط

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms in rhetorical speech omit obvious intermediate conclusions; the trained logician can restore the hidden structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Identify the minor, middle, and major terms in the Prophet's saying: 'Actions are judged by intentions.' (Hint: it can be analyzed as a syllogism with 'intention' as the middle term.)

The Syllogism
beginner

Gold Purity Analogy and Dialectical Syllogism
Analogy explaining syllogism classification by premise certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the gold purity analogy to classify syllogisms by premise certainty and identify the dialectical syllogism.

Core Idea
The certainty of premises determines a syllogism's value, like gold purity determines a coin's worth.

Al-Ghazali draws a powerful analogy between gold coins and syllogisms. Just as a dinar's value depends on the purity of its gold, a syllogism's epistemic value depends on the certainty of its premises. This lesson unpacks that analogy to reveal the four types of syllogisms, with special attention to the dialectical syllogism used in scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy: matter and form of a dinar
Al-Ghazali states that the round shape and engraving (form) are additional to the pure gold (matter). Similarly, a syllogism has matter (premises) and form (mood/figure). Identify this parallel before moving to purity.
2
List the four degrees of gold purity
From the passage, extract the four states: (1) pure gold, no adulteration; (2) gold close to pure but not highest; (3) gold with much adulteration (silver/copper); (4) not gold at all, imitation. Write these down.
The author explicitly names only the first two, but the pattern implies four.
3
Map each gold state to a type of syllogism
Al-Ghazali says 'the beliefs that are the materials of syllogisms' follow similar degrees. Map: (1) certain premises → demonstrative (burhani); (2) premises close to certainty → dialectical (jadali); (3) probable premises → rhetorical (khitabi) or sophistical?; (4) false premises → fallacious (safsati). Note the passage explicitly defines only the second.
The full four-arts classification is standard: burhan, jadal, khitaba, safsata.
4
Focus on the dialectical syllogism
Al-Ghazali describes the second degree: belief close to certainty, accepted by the public, not immediately questioned but requiring subtle thought. The resulting syllogism is called 'jadali' because it suits disputations. Mark this as the key concept introduced.
Dialectical syllogisms are used in munazara (debate) where premises are mashhoorat (widely accepted).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كما أن صورة الاستدارة والنقش للدينار زائد على مادة الدينار، فإن المادة للدينار هي الذهب الإبريز، فكذا في القياس، وكما أن الذهب الذي هو الدينار له أربعة أحوال : أعلاها أن يكون ذهباً خالصاً إبريزاً لا غش فيه أصلاً. والثانية : أن يكون ذهباً مقارناً لا في غاية رتبته العليا، ولا كذلك الذهب الإبريز الخالص. والثالثة : أن يكون ذهباً كثير الغش لاختلاط النقرة والنحاس به . والرابعة : أن لا يكون ذهباً أصلاً بل يكون جنساً على حدة مشبهاً بالذهب أنواع القياس فكذلك الاعتقادات التي هي مواد الأقيسة قد تكون اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين مقبولاً عند الكافة في الظاهر، لا يشعر الذهن بإمكان نقيضه على الفور بل بدقيق الفكر، فيسمى القياس المؤلف منه جدلياً إذ يصلح لمناظرات الخصوم وقد يكون اعتقاداً بحيث لا يقع به تصديق جزم، ولكن غالب ظن وقناعة نفس مع خطور نقيضه بالبال أو قبول النفس لنقيضه إن خطر بالبال وإن وقعت الغفلة عنه في أكثر
Then just as the form of roundness and engraving on the dinar is additional to the matter of the dinar—for the matter of the dinar is pure gold—so too in the syllogism. And just as gold that is a dinar has four states: the highest is that it is pure gold without any adulteration; the second is that it is gold that is close but not in its highest rank, and not like the pure gold; the third is that it is gold with much adulteration due to mixing of silver and copper; and the fourth is that it is not gold at all but a separate genus resembling gold. Types of syllogisms. Similarly, the beliefs that are the materials of syllogisms may be a belief close to certainty, accepted by the public in appearance, the mind not immediately sensing the possibility of its opposite but with subtle thought, so the syllogism composed of it is called dialectical (jadali) because it is suitable for disputations with opponents. And it may be a belief such that no decisive assent occurs from it, but rather it is [merely] prevalent opinion and satisfaction of the soul while the possibility of its opposite occurs to the mind, or the soul accepts its opposite if it occurs, even if it is heedless of it most [of the time].
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the matter of the syllogism by using a striking analogy from coinage. Just as a gold coin (dinar) has a material substance (pure gold) and a form (round, stamped), so does a syllogism have matter (premises) and form. The author then lists four degrees of gold purity, from pure gold to a mere imitation. He parallels this with four degrees of belief that serve as premises for syllogisms. He explicitly names only one type so far: a belief close to certainty but not fully certain, which yields the dialectical syllogism (qiyas jadali) used in debate. The passage cuts off before describing the other types, but the intention is to map to the four degrees of gold. This classification is crucial for the five arts: demonstration (burhan) corresponds to pure gold (certainty), dialectic to the second degree (approximate certainty), and so on for rhetoric, sophistry, and poetics. The author's emphasis is that not all syllogisms produce certain knowledge; the value of the conclusion depends on the quality of the premises.

Key Terms
جدلي (jadali): Dialectical syllogism; based on premises that are generally accepted (mashhoorat) or close to certainty, used in debate. [Note: Al-Ghazali specifies the premise type as 'muqarib lil-yaqin' (close to certainty), which aligns with the standard definition.]
اعتقاد مقارب لليقين (iʿtiqād muqārib lil-yaqīn): Belief close to certainty; the matter of dialectical syllogisms, accepted commonly but not fully certain. [Note: No significant shift.]
أنواع القياس (anwāʿ al-qiyās): Types of syllogisms; classification based on the certainty of the premises, analogous to gold purity. [Note: Al-Ghazali's four-type classification is standard in later works.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ghazali's analogy, which type of syllogism corresponds to gold that is pure but not in the highest rank?
A) Demonstrative (burhani)
B) Dialectical (jadali)
C) Rhetorical (khitabi)
D) Sophistical (safsati)

Key Takeaway
The certainty of a syllogism's premises determines its type: dialectical syllogisms use premises close to certainty but not absolute, making them suitable for debate but not for establishing certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a dialectical syllogism on a fiqh issue. Use a premise that is commonly accepted among scholars (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden') but not necessarily certain from revelation. Then complete the syllogism and identify why it is dialectical, not demonstrative.
?
Identify whether the following syllogism is dialectical or demonstrative: 'The world is temporally originated; every temporally originated thing requires a cause; therefore the world requires a cause.' Note the status of the premise about 'temporally originated' – is it certain or generally accepted? Explain.

The Five Syllogistic Arts: Classification by Premise Quality
Author classifies syllogisms into five types based on epistemic quality of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five types of syllogisms based on the epistemic quality of their premises as described in the passage.

Core Idea
The material of a syllogism determines its truth-value and purpose.

Logic classifies syllogisms not only by form but also by the type of premises they use. The author here presents a classification based on how certain, accepted, persuasive, fallacious, or imaginative the premises are. Understanding these types is essential for applying logic correctly in different Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types
List the five syllogism types mentioned: demonstrative (implied), dialectical, rhetorical, sophistical, and poetic. Note that demonstrative is not described here but is the first rank.
The passage explicitly describes dialectical, rhetorical, sophistical, and poetic.
2
Analyze the epistemic quality of each type
For each type, identify the kind of belief its premises produce: dialectical premises approximate certainty, rhetorical produce dominant opinion, sophistical mimic certainty falsely, poetic produce emotional effect.
Demonstrative premises produce absolute certainty (yaqīn).
3
Determine the purpose of each type
Each type serves a specific purpose: dialectical for disputation, rhetorical for teaching and addresses, sophistical for deception, poetic for emotional influence, demonstrative for proof.
4
Apply the classification to examples
Consider a given argument and decide which type it belongs to by examining the strength and nature of its premises.
The passage gives poetic examples; practice with other Islamic contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس فكذلك الاعتقادات التي هي مواد الأقيسة قد تكون اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين مقبولاً عند الكافة في الظاهر، لا يشعر الذهن بإمكان نقيضه على الفور بل بدقيق الفكر، فيسمى القياس المؤلف منه جدلياً إذ يصلح لمناظرات الخصوم وقد يكون اعتقاداً بحيث لا يقع به تصديق جزم، ولكن غالب ظن وقناعة نفس مع خطور نقيضه بالبال أو قبول النفس لنقيضه إن خطر بالبال وإن وقعت الغفلة عنه في أكثر الأحوال، ويُسمّى القياس المؤلف منه خطابياً إذ يصلح للإيراد في التعليمات والمخاطبات، وقد يكون تارةً مشبهاً باليقين أو بالمشهور المقارب لليقين في الظاهر وليس بالحقيقة كذلك، وهو الجهل المحض، ويسمى القياس المؤلف منه مغالطياً وسوفسطائياً إذ لا يقصد بذلك إلَّا المغالطة والسفسطة، وهو إبطال الحقائق ؛ فهذه أربع مراتب لا بد من تمييز البعض منها عن البعض . وأما الخامس الذي يسمى قياساً شعرياً فليس يدخل في غرضنا فإنه لا يذكر لإفادة علم أو ظن، بل المخاطب قد يعلم حقيقته، وإنما يذكر لترغيب أو تنفير أو تسخية أو تبخيل أو ترهيب أو تشجيع وله تأثير في النفس بترديدها على هذه الأحوال وإيجابه انقباضاً وانبساطاً مع معرفة بطلانه . وذلك كنفرة الطبع عن الحلو الأصفر إذا شبه بالعَذِرة حتى يتعذر في الحال تناولها وإن علم كذب قائله، وعليه تعويل صناعة الشعر وبه تشبث أكثر المُتشدّقين من الوعاظ، فإنهم يستعملون في النثر صناعة الشعر، ومثاله أن من يريد أن يحمل غيره على التهوّر ، ويصرفه عن الحزم يلقب الحزم بالجبن ويقبّحه ويذمّ صاحبه فيقول : [من الوافر] يَرَى الجبناء أنّ الجُبْنَ حَزْمٌ وَتِلْكَ خَديعةُ النَّفس اللئ... فتنبسط نفس المتوقف إلى التهجّم بذلك، كقوله: [من الطويل] إذا لم أَمُتْ تحتَ السّيوفِ مُكرّماً أُمُتْ وأقاسي الذُّلَّ غَيْرَ مُكرَّم وكذلك إذا أراد التسخية أطنب في مدح السّخي وشبهه بما يعلم أنه لا يُشْبِهُهُ، ولكن يُؤثر في نفسه كقوله : [من الطويل] هو الـبـحـر مـن أي الجوانب جِئْتَهُ فَلُجَّتُهُ المعروف والجودُ سَاحِلُه
The second consideration: on the matter of the syllogism. Likewise, the beliefs that are the materials of syllogisms may be a belief approximating certainty, accepted by the common people in appearance, the mind not perceiving the possibility of its opposite immediately but by subtle thought, and the syllogism composed of it is called dialectical because it is suitable for disputations with opponents. And it may be a belief such that no decisive assent occurs by it, but rather a dominant opinion and satisfaction of the soul, with its opposite occurring to the mind or the soul accepting its opposite if it occurs, even if it is heedless of it in most cases, and the syllogism composed of it is called rhetorical because it is suitable for use in teachings and addresses. And it may sometimes be similar to certainty or to the accepted approximation of certainty in appearance but not in reality, and it is pure ignorance, and the syllogism composed of it is called sophistical, because nothing is intended by it but sophistry and fallacy, and it is the nullification of truths. So these are four ranks that must be distinguished from each other. As for the fifth, which is called poetic syllogism, it does not enter into our purpose, for it is not mentioned to produce knowledge or opinion; rather, the addressee may know its truth, but it is mentioned to induce desire, aversion, generosity, miserliness, fear, or courage, and it has an effect on the soul by moving it among these states and causing contraction and expansion while knowing its falsehood. An example is the natural aversion to yellow sweets when compared to feces, such that immediate consumption becomes difficult even if one knows the speaker is lying. Upon this depends the craft of poetry, and with it most of the verbose preachers cling, for they use the craft of poetry in prose. An example: one who wants to incite recklessness and turn another from caution calls caution cowardice and denigrates it and blames its possessor, saying (from al-wafir): 'The cowards see that cowardice is caution, and that is a deception of the vile soul.' Then the hesitating soul expands towards recklessness by that. Another example (from al-tawil): 'If I do not die under swords honorably, I will die enduring humiliation without honor.' And likewise, if he wants to incite generosity, he exaggerates in praising the generous and compares him to what he knows does not resemble him, but it affects the soul, as he says (from al-tawil): 'He is the sea; from whichever side you approach it, its depth is generosity and its shore is magnanimity.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogisms based on the epistemic quality of their premises. The author identifies four main types plus a fifth (poetic) that is not his focus. The first is demonstrative (yaqīni), based on certain premises; the second is dialectical (jadalī), based on premises approximating certainty and commonly accepted; the third is rhetorical (khiṭābī), based on opinion and persuasion; the fourth is sophistical (mughāliṭī/sūfastāʾī), based on fallacious premises that appear certain but are not. The fifth is poetic (shiʿrī), which uses imaginative language to affect emotions without aiming at truth. The author gives examples from Arabic poetry, showing how verses can encourage recklessness or generosity through imagery. This classification is crucial for understanding the five arts (ṣināʿāt) in logic.

Key Terms
قياس جدلي
قياس خطابي
قياس مغالطي
قياس شعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of syllogism is used in disputations with opponents?
A) Demonstrative
B) Dialectical
C) Rhetorical
D) Sophistical

Key Takeaway
Syllogisms are classified into five arts based on the epistemic quality of their premises, from certain to imaginative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the type of syllogism in the following argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' The premises are accepted by Muslims and produce certainty; it is demonstrative.
?
Classify this argument from a preacher: 'If you give charity, your wealth will increase. Generous people are loved by Allah.' The premises aim to persuade and produce dominant opinion; it is rhetorical.

The Syllogism

The Dialectical Syllogism (Qiyās Jadalī): Premises Approximating Certainty
Author defines dialectical syllogism and its premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the dialectical syllogism and identify its premises as those approximating certainty and accepted by the public.

Core Idea
Dialectical syllogisms use commonly accepted premises that seem certain at first glance.

Among the five syllogistic arts, the dialectical syllogism (qiyās jadalī) occupies a middle ground between demonstrative certainty and rhetorical persuasion. Its premises are not fully certain but are accepted by all as truth-like, making them useful for formal debates. The passage describes it as 'a belief approximating certainty, accepted by the common people in appearance.'

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of dialectical syllogism
Find the part of the passage where the author describes the first type of belief: 'a belief approximating certainty, accepted by the common people in appearance.' This is the material of the dialectical syllogism.
The Arabic phrase is 'اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين مقبولاً عند الكافة'.
2
Identify the epistemic nature
Note that this belief is not fully certain: the mind does not perceive its opposite immediately but only after subtle thought. This distinguishes it from demonstrative certainty.
The opposite is possible but not obvious.
3
Recognize the purpose
The passage says the syllogism composed of such premises is called dialectical 'because it is suitable for disputations with opponents' (يصلح لمناظرات الخصوم).
It is used in jadal (dialectical debates).
4
Distinguish from other types
Contrast with rhetorical (dominant opinion only) and sophistical (appears certain but false). The dialectical premises are genuinely accepted and nearly certain.
They are not as strong as demonstrative but stronger than rhetorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس فكذلك الاعتقادات التي هي مواد الأقيسة قد تكون اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين مقبولاً عند الكافة في الظاهر، لا يشعر الذهن بإمكان نقيضه على الفور بل بدقيق الفكر، فيسمى القياس المؤلف منه جدلياً إذ يصلح لمناظرات الخصوم وقد يكون اعتقاداً بحيث لا يقع به تصديق جزم، ولكن غالب ظن وقناعة نفس مع خطور نقيضه بالبال أو قبول النفس لنقيضه إن خطر بالبال وإن وقعت الغفلة عنه في أكثر الأحوال، ويُسمّى القياس المؤلف منه خطابياً إذ يصلح للإيراد في التعليمات والمخاطبات، وقد يكون تارةً مشبهاً باليقين أو بالمشهور المقارب لليقين في الظاهر وليس بالحقيقة كذلك، وهو الجهل المحض، ويسمى القياس المؤلف منه مغالطياً وسوفسطائياً إذ لا يقصد بذلك إلَّا المغالطة والسفسطة، وهو إبطال الحقائق ؛ فهذه أربع مراتب لا بد من تمييز البعض منها عن البعض . وأما الخامس الذي يسمى قياساً شعرياً فليس يدخل في غرضنا فإنه لا يذكر لإفادة علم أو ظن، بل المخاطب قد يعلم حقيقته، وإنما يذكر لترغيب أو تنفير أو تسخية أو تبخيل أو ترهيب أو تشجيع وله تأثير في النفس بترديدها على هذه الأحوال وإيجابه انقباضاً وانبساطاً مع معرفة بطلانه . وذلك كنفرة الطبع عن الحلو الأصفر إذا شبه بالعَذِرة حتى يتعذر في الحال تناولها وإن علم كذب قائله، وعليه تعويل صناعة الشعر وبه تشبث أكثر المُتشدّقين من الوعاظ، فإنهم يستعملون في النثر صناعة الشعر، ومثاله أن من يريد أن يحمل غيره على التهوّر ، ويصرفه عن الحزم يلقب الحزم بالجبن ويقبّحه ويذمّ صاحبه فيقول : [من الوافر] يَرَى الجبناء أنّ الجُبْنَ حَزْمٌ وَتِلْكَ خَديعةُ النَّفس اللئ... فتنبسط نفس المتوقف إلى التهجّم بذلك، كقوله: [من الطويل] إذا لم أَمُتْ تحتَ السّيوفِ مُكرّماً أُمُتْ وأقاسي الذُّلَّ غَيْرَ مُكرَّم وكذلك إذا أراد التسخية أطنب في مدح السّخي وشبهه بما يعلم أنه لا يُشْبِهُهُ، ولكن يُؤثر في نفسه كقوله : [من الطويل] هو الـبـحـر مـن أي الجوانب جِئْتَهُ فَلُجَّتُهُ المعروف والجودُ سَاحِلُه
The second consideration: on the matter of the syllogism. Likewise, the beliefs that are the materials of syllogisms may be a belief approximating certainty, accepted by the common people in appearance, the mind not perceiving the possibility of its opposite immediately but by subtle thought, and the syllogism composed of it is called dialectical because it is suitable for disputations with opponents. And it may be a belief such that no decisive assent occurs by it, but rather a dominant opinion and satisfaction of the soul, with its opposite occurring to the mind or the soul accepting its opposite if it occurs, even if it is heedless of it in most cases, and the syllogism composed of it is called rhetorical because it is suitable for use in teachings and addresses. And it may sometimes be similar to certainty or to the accepted approximation of certainty in appearance but not in reality, and it is pure ignorance, and the syllogism composed of it is called sophistical, because nothing is intended by it but sophistry and fallacy, and it is the nullification of truths. So these are four ranks that must be distinguished from each other. As for the fifth, which is called poetic syllogism, it does not enter into our purpose, for it is not mentioned to produce knowledge or opinion; rather, the addressee may know its truth, but it is mentioned to induce desire, aversion, generosity, miserliness, fear, or courage, and it has an effect on the soul by moving it among these states and causing contraction and expansion while knowing its falsehood. An example is the natural aversion to yellow sweets when compared to feces, such that immediate consumption becomes difficult even if one knows the speaker is lying. Upon this depends the craft of poetry, and with it most of the verbose preachers cling, for they use the craft of poetry in prose. An example: one who wants to incite recklessness and turn another from caution calls caution cowardice and denigrates it and blames its possessor, saying (from al-wafir): 'The cowards see that cowardice is caution, and that is a deception of the vile soul.' Then the hesitating soul expands towards recklessness by that. Another example (from al-tawil): 'If I do not die under swords honorably, I will die enduring humiliation without honor.' And likewise, if he wants to incite generosity, he exaggerates in praising the generous and compares him to what he knows does not resemble him, but it affects the soul, as he says (from al-tawil): 'He is the sea; from whichever side you approach it, its depth is generosity and its shore is magnanimity.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogisms based on the epistemic quality of their premises. The author identifies four main types plus a fifth (poetic) that is not his focus. The first is demonstrative (yaqīni), based on certain premises; the second is dialectical (jadalī), based on premises approximating certainty and commonly accepted; the third is rhetorical (khiṭābī), based on opinion and persuasion; the fourth is sophistical (mughāliṭī/sūfastāʾī), based on fallacious premises that appear certain but are not. The fifth is poetic (shiʿrī), which uses imaginative language to affect emotions without aiming at truth. The author gives examples from Arabic poetry, showing how verses can encourage recklessness or generosity through imagery. This classification is crucial for understanding the five arts (ṣināʿāt) in logic.

مقارب لليقين
مقبول عند الكافة

The Rhetorical Syllogism (Qiyās Khiṭābī): Persuasion Through Dominant Opinion
Author defines rhetorical syllogism and its premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the rhetorical syllogism and distinguish its premises from those of dialectical and demonstrative syllogisms.

Core Idea
Rhetorical syllogisms persuade by producing dominant opinion, not certainty.

The rhetorical syllogism (qiyās khiṭābī) is designed to sway an audience by creating a strong opinion, even if certainty is absent. Its premises yield ‘dominant opinion and satisfaction of the soul,’ but the opposite remains conceivable. The passage explains that it is used in teachings and addresses (khitābāt).

Guided Path
1
Find the description of rhetorical belief
Look for the phrase: 'a belief such that no decisive assent occurs by it, but rather a dominant opinion and satisfaction of the soul.' This characterizes the material of rhetorical syllogism.
The Arabic: 'اعتقاداً بحيث لا يقع به تصديق جزم، ولكن غالب ظن وقناعة نفس'.
2
Understand the epistemic weakness
Note that the opposite is possible and even accepted if it occurs. This makes it weaker than dialectical (where opposite is not immediately noticed).
The soul may be heedless of the opposite in most cases.
3
Identify the purpose
The syllogism is called rhetorical 'because it is suitable for use in teachings and addresses' (يصلح للإيراد في التعليمات والمخاطبات).
It aims at persuasion, not proof.
4
Contrast with other types
Rhetorical premises are less certain than dialectical but more than poetic; they aim at belief rather than imagination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس فكذلك الاعتقادات التي هي مواد الأقيسة قد تكون اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين مقبولاً عند الكافة في الظاهر، لا يشعر الذهن بإمكان نقيضه على الفور بل بدقيق الفكر، فيسمى القياس المؤلف منه جدلياً إذ يصلح لمناظرات الخصوم وقد يكون اعتقاداً بحيث لا يقع به تصديق جزم، ولكن غالب ظن وقناعة نفس مع خطور نقيضه بالبال أو قبول النفس لنقيضه إن خطر بالبال وإن وقعت الغفلة عنه في أكثر الأحوال، ويُسمّى القياس المؤلف منه خطابياً إذ يصلح للإيراد في التعليمات والمخاطبات، وقد يكون تارةً مشبهاً باليقين أو بالمشهور المقارب لليقين في الظاهر وليس بالحقيقة كذلك، وهو الجهل المحض، ويسمى القياس المؤلف منه مغالطياً وسوفسطائياً إذ لا يقصد بذلك إلَّا المغالطة والسفسطة، وهو إبطال الحقائق ؛ فهذه أربع مراتب لا بد من تمييز البعض منها عن البعض . وأما الخامس الذي يسمى قياساً شعرياً فليس يدخل في غرضنا فإنه لا يذكر لإفادة علم أو ظن، بل المخاطب قد يعلم حقيقته، وإنما يذكر لترغيب أو تنفير أو تسخية أو تبخيل أو ترهيب أو تشجيع وله تأثير في النفس بترديدها على هذه الأحوال وإيجابه انقباضاً وانبساطاً مع معرفة بطلانه . وذلك كنفرة الطبع عن الحلو الأصفر إذا شبه بالعَذِرة حتى يتعذر في الحال تناولها وإن علم كذب قائله، وعليه تعويل صناعة الشعر وبه تشبث أكثر المُتشدّقين من الوعاظ، فإنهم يستعملون في النثر صناعة الشعر، ومثاله أن من يريد أن يحمل غيره على التهوّر ، ويصرفه عن الحزم يلقب الحزم بالجبن ويقبّحه ويذمّ صاحبه فيقول : [من الوافر] يَرَى الجبناء أنّ الجُبْنَ حَزْمٌ وَتِلْكَ خَديعةُ النَّفس اللئ... فتنبسط نفس المتوقف إلى التهجّم بذلك، كقوله: [من الطويل] إذا لم أَمُتْ تحتَ السّيوفِ مُكرّماً أُمُتْ وأقاسي الذُّلَّ غَيْرَ مُكرَّم وكذلك إذا أراد التسخية أطنب في مدح السّخي وشبهه بما يعلم أنه لا يُشْبِهُهُ، ولكن يُؤثر في نفسه كقوله : [من الطويل] هو الـبـحـر مـن أي الجوانب جِئْتَهُ فَلُجَّتُهُ المعروف والجودُ سَاحِلُه
The second consideration: on the matter of the syllogism. Likewise, the beliefs that are the materials of syllogisms may be a belief approximating certainty, accepted by the common people in appearance, the mind not perceiving the possibility of its opposite immediately but by subtle thought, and the syllogism composed of it is called dialectical because it is suitable for disputations with opponents. And it may be a belief such that no decisive assent occurs by it, but rather a dominant opinion and satisfaction of the soul, with its opposite occurring to the mind or the soul accepting its opposite if it occurs, even if it is heedless of it in most cases, and the syllogism composed of it is called rhetorical because it is suitable for use in teachings and addresses. And it may sometimes be similar to certainty or to the accepted approximation of certainty in appearance but not in reality, and it is pure ignorance, and the syllogism composed of it is called sophistical, because nothing is intended by it but sophistry and fallacy, and it is the nullification of truths. So these are four ranks that must be distinguished from each other. As for the fifth, which is called poetic syllogism, it does not enter into our purpose, for it is not mentioned to produce knowledge or opinion; rather, the addressee may know its truth, but it is mentioned to induce desire, aversion, generosity, miserliness, fear, or courage, and it has an effect on the soul by moving it among these states and causing contraction and expansion while knowing its falsehood. An example is the natural aversion to yellow sweets when compared to feces, such that immediate consumption becomes difficult even if one knows the speaker is lying. Upon this depends the craft of poetry, and with it most of the verbose preachers cling, for they use the craft of poetry in prose. An example: one who wants to incite recklessness and turn another from caution calls caution cowardice and denigrates it and blames its possessor, saying (from al-wafir): 'The cowards see that cowardice is caution, and that is a deception of the vile soul.' Then the hesitating soul expands towards recklessness by that. Another example (from al-tawil): 'If I do not die under swords honorably, I will die enduring humiliation without honor.' And likewise, if he wants to incite generosity, he exaggerates in praising the generous and compares him to what he knows does not resemble him, but it affects the soul, as he says (from al-tawil): 'He is the sea; from whichever side you approach it, its depth is generosity and its shore is magnanimity.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogisms based on the epistemic quality of their premises. The author identifies four main types plus a fifth (poetic) that is not his focus. The first is demonstrative (yaqīni), based on certain premises; the second is dialectical (jadalī), based on premises approximating certainty and commonly accepted; the third is rhetorical (khiṭābī), based on opinion and persuasion; the fourth is sophistical (mughāliṭī/sūfastāʾī), based on fallacious premises that appear certain but are not. The fifth is poetic (shiʿrī), which uses imaginative language to affect emotions without aiming at truth. The author gives examples from Arabic poetry, showing how verses can encourage recklessness or generosity through imagery. This classification is crucial for understanding the five arts (ṣināʿāt) in logic.

Key Terms
قياس خطابي
غالب ظن
قناعة نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What kind of belief does a rhetorical syllogism produce according to the passage?
A) Absolute certainty
B) Dominant opinion and satisfaction of the soul
C) Complete doubt
D) Imaginative impression

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical syllogisms produce strong belief but not certainty, used in teachings and addresses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example of a rhetorical syllogism in a Friday sermon. Identify the premises and explain why they produce dominant opinion rather than certainty.
?
Contrast a rhetorical syllogism with a dialectical one: for the claim 'Zakat is obligatory', give a rhetorical premise (e.g., 'The Quran commands it' – produces dominant opinion) and a dialectical premise (e.g., 'It is one of the five pillars' – accepted by all Muslims).

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Sophistical Syllogism (Qiyās Mughāliṭī): Deceptive Arguments
Author defines sophistical syllogism and its deceptive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the sophistical syllogism and identify its characteristics: premises that appear certain but are false.

Core Idea
Sophistical syllogisms mimic certainty to deceive, but they are pure ignorance.

The sophistical syllogism (qiyās mughāliṭī) is the tool of sophistry. Its premises resemble certainty or accepted opinion but are actually false. The author calls it 'pure ignorance' and 'the nullification of truths.' Understanding this type is crucial for avoiding deception in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the description of sophistical belief
Find the sentence: 'it may sometimes be similar to certainty or to the accepted approximation of certainty in appearance but not in reality, and it is pure ignorance.' This defines the material of sophistical syllogism.
Arabic: 'مشبهاً باليقين أو بالمشهور المقارب لليقين في الظاهر وليس بالحقيقة كذلك'.
2
Understand the deceitful nature
The premises are designed to appear true, but they are false. The syllogism is used only for sophistry and fallacy, aiming to nullify truths.
It is intentional deception.
3
Recognize the lack of epistemic value
Unlike dialectical or rhetorical, these premises give no genuine belief; they are 'pure ignorance' (الجهل المحض).
They are not accepted in reality.
4
Identify typical examples
Think of fallacies like equivocation or circular reasoning that look valid but are not. The passage warns that this syllogism is used to undermine truth.
The author equates it with 'sophistics' (سفسطة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس فكذلك الاعتقادات التي هي مواد الأقيسة قد تكون اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين مقبولاً عند الكافة في الظاهر، لا يشعر الذهن بإمكان نقيضه على الفور بل بدقيق الفكر، فيسمى القياس المؤلف منه جدلياً إذ يصلح لمناظرات الخصوم وقد يكون اعتقاداً بحيث لا يقع به تصديق جزم، ولكن غالب ظن وقناعة نفس مع خطور نقيضه بالبال أو قبول النفس لنقيضه إن خطر بالبال وإن وقعت الغفلة عنه في أكثر الأحوال، ويُسمّى القياس المؤلف منه خطابياً إذ يصلح للإيراد في التعليمات والمخاطبات، وقد يكون تارةً مشبهاً باليقين أو بالمشهور المقارب لليقين في الظاهر وليس بالحقيقة كذلك، وهو الجهل المحض، ويسمى القياس المؤلف منه مغالطياً وسوفسطائياً إذ لا يقصد بذلك إلَّا المغالطة والسفسطة، وهو إبطال الحقائق ؛ فهذه أربع مراتب لا بد من تمييز البعض منها عن البعض . وأما الخامس الذي يسمى قياساً شعرياً فليس يدخل في غرضنا فإنه لا يذكر لإفادة علم أو ظن، بل المخاطب قد يعلم حقيقته، وإنما يذكر لترغيب أو تنفير أو تسخية أو تبخيل أو ترهيب أو تشجيع وله تأثير في النفس بترديدها على هذه الأحوال وإيجابه انقباضاً وانبساطاً مع معرفة بطلانه . وذلك كنفرة الطبع عن الحلو الأصفر إذا شبه بالعَذِرة حتى يتعذر في الحال تناولها وإن علم كذب قائله، وعليه تعويل صناعة الشعر وبه تشبث أكثر المُتشدّقين من الوعاظ، فإنهم يستعملون في النثر صناعة الشعر، ومثاله أن من يريد أن يحمل غيره على التهوّر ، ويصرفه عن الحزم يلقب الحزم بالجبن ويقبّحه ويذمّ صاحبه فيقول : [من الوافر] يَرَى الجبناء أنّ الجُبْنَ حَزْمٌ وَتِلْكَ خَديعةُ النَّفس اللئ... فتنبسط نفس المتوقف إلى التهجّم بذلك، كقوله: [من الطويل] إذا لم أَمُتْ تحتَ السّيوفِ مُكرّماً أُمُتْ وأقاسي الذُّلَّ غَيْرَ مُكرَّم وكذلك إذا أراد التسخية أطنب في مدح السّخي وشبهه بما يعلم أنه لا يُشْبِهُهُ، ولكن يُؤثر في نفسه كقوله : [من الطويل] هو الـبـحـر مـن أي الجوانب جِئْتَهُ فَلُجَّتُهُ المعروف والجودُ سَاحِلُه
The second consideration: on the matter of the syllogism. Likewise, the beliefs that are the materials of syllogisms may be a belief approximating certainty, accepted by the common people in appearance, the mind not perceiving the possibility of its opposite immediately but by subtle thought, and the syllogism composed of it is called dialectical because it is suitable for disputations with opponents. The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
The Poetic Syllogism (Qiyās Shiʿrī): Imagination Over Truth
Author defines poetic syllogism and explains its emotional effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the poetic syllogism and explain how it affects the soul through imaginative premises without aiming at truth.

Core Idea
Poetic syllogisms influence emotions using imagery, even when the listener knows the content is false.

The poetic syllogism (qiyās shiʿrī) is the fifth type, but the author says it is not his main subject. Unlike the other four, it does not aim to produce knowledge or opinion. Instead, it uses imaginative comparisons to induce emotional responses such as desire, aversion, generosity, or fear. The listener may know the claim is false, yet the soul is still moved.

Guided Path
1
Locate the description of poetic syllogism
Find the part: 'the fifth, which is called poetic syllogism, does not enter into our purpose, for it is not mentioned to produce knowledge or opinion...' This sets it apart from the other types.
Arabic: 'وأما الخامس الذي يسمى قياساً شعرياً'.
2
Understand its purpose
Its purpose is emotional: to induce desire, aversion, generosity, miserliness, fear, or courage. It works through imaginative representation (takhayyul).
The author lists six effects: ترغيب، تنفير، تسخية، تبخيل، ترهيب، تشجيع.
3
Analyze the example of yellow sweets
The author gives an example: comparing yellow sweets to feces causes aversion, even though the listener knows the comparison is false. The soul contracts and expansion is prevented.
This shows the power of imaginative over factual.
4
Recognize its use in poetry and preaching
The craft of poetry relies on this, and verbose preachers use it in prose to move audiences. The quoted verses illustrate how calling caution 'cowardice' incites recklessness.
The examples show how language can reshape values temporarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس فكذلك الاعتقادات التي هي مواد الأقيسة قد تكون اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين مقبولاً عند الكافة في الظاهر، لا يشعر الذهن بإمكان نقيضه على الفور بل بدقيق الفكر، فيسمى القياس المؤلف منه جدلياً إذ يصلح لمناظرات الخصوم وقد يكون اعتقاداً بحيث لا يقع به تصديق جزم، ولكن غالب ظن وقناعة نفس مع خطور نقيضه بالبال أو قبول النفس لنقيضه إن خطر بالبال وإن وقعت الغفلة عنه في أكثر الأحوال، ويُسمّى القياس المؤلف منه خطابياً إذ يصلح للإيراد في التعليمات والمخاطبات، وقد يكون تارةً مشبهاً باليقين أو بالمشهور المقارب لليقين في الظاهر وليس بالحقيقة كذلك، وهو الجهل المحض، ويسمى القياس المؤلف منه مغالطياً وسوفسطائياً إذ لا يقصد بذلك إلَّا المغالطة والسفسطة، وهو إبطال الحقائق ؛ فهذه أربع مراتب لا بد من تمييز البعض منها عن البعض . وأما الخامس الذي يسمى قياساً شعرياً فليس يدخل في غرضنا فإنه لا يذكر لإفادة علم أو ظن، بل المخاطب قد يعلم حقيقته، وإنما يذكر لترغيب أو تنفير أو تسخية أو تبخيل أو ترهيب أو تشجيع وله تأثير في النفس بترديدها على هذه الأحوال وإيجابه انقباضاً وانبساطاً مع معرفة بطلانه . وذلك كنفرة الطبع عن الحلو الأصفر إذا شبه بالعَذِرة حتى يتعذر في الحال تناولها وإن علم كذب قائله، وعليه تعويل صناعة الشعر وبه تشبث أكثر المُتشدّقين من الوعاظ، فإنهم يستعملون في النثر صناعة الشعر، ومثاله أن من يريد أن يحمل غيره على التهوّر ، ويصرفه عن الحزم يلقب الحزم بالجبن ويقبّحه ويذمّ صاحبه فيقول : [من الوافر] يَرَى الجبناء أنّ الجُبْنَ حَزْمٌ وَتِلْكَ خَديعةُ النَّفس اللئ... فتنبسط نفس المتوقف إلى التهجّم بذلك، كقوله: [من الطويل] إذا لم أَمُتْ تحتَ السّيوفِ مُكرّماً أُمُتْ وأقاسي الذُّلَّ غَيْرَ مُكرَّم وكذلك إذا أراد التسخية أطنب في مدح السّخي وشبهه بما يعلم أنه لا يُشْبِهُهُ، ولكن يُؤثر في نفسه كقوله : [من الطويل] هو الـبـحـر مـن أي الجوانب جِئْتَهُ فَلُجَّتُهُ المعروف والجودُ سَاحِلُه
The second consideration: on the matter of the syllogism. Likewise, the beliefs that are the materials of syllogisms may be a belief approximating certainty, accepted by the common people in appearance, the mind not perceiving the possibility of its opposite immediately but by subtle thought, and the syllogism composed of it is called dialectical because it is suitable for disputations with opponents. So these are four ranks that must be distinguished from each other. Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogisms based on the epistemic quality of their premises. The author identifies four main types plus a fifth (poetic) that is not his focus. The first is demonstrative (yaqīni), based on certain premises; the second is dialectical (jadalī), based on premises approximating certainty and commonly accepted; the third is rhetorical (khiṭābī), based on opinion and persuasion; the fourth is sophistical (mughāliṭī/sūfastāʾī), based on fallacious premises that appear certain but are not. The fifth is poetic (shiʿrī), which uses imaginative language to affect emotions without aiming at truth. The author gives examples from Arabic poetry, showing how verses can encourage recklessness or generosity through imagery. This classification is crucial for understanding the five arts (ṣināʿāt) in logic.

Key Terms
قياس شعري
ترغيب
تنفير
تخييل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of a poetic syllogism on the soul?
A) It produces certain knowledge about the subject.
B) It creates dominant opinion and satisfaction.
C) It causes contraction and expansion (emotional movement) while knowing the falsehood.
D) It leads to intellectual deception.

Key Takeaway
Poetic syllogisms use imaginative premises to move the soul emotionally, not to convey truth.

The Syllogism

The Four Syllogisms of Logical Inquiry
Author classifies syllogisms into five types by their matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of syllogism (demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, sophistical) based on their premises and purpose.

Core Idea
Not all syllogisms aim at truth—only the demonstrative does.

In this passage, the author classifies syllogisms by the kind of premises they use. Understanding these four types is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, where the certainty of premises determines the value of a proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four ranks
List the four types mentioned in the passage in order of decreasing certainty: demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, sophistical.
The sophistical is considered 'pure ignorance' because it mimics truth.
2
Match each rank to its Arabic term
Connect the English names with the Arabic terms: برهاني, جدلي, خطابي, مغالطي. Note that each term reflects the discipline it serves (burhan, jadal, khataba, mughalata).
3
Extract the purpose of each type
From the passage, determine what each syllogism is used for: knowledge (burhani), dialectic (jadali), persuasion (khatabi), or sophistry (mughaliti).
The rhetorical is used in 'instructions and addresses'.
4
Distinguish the sophistical from the others
The sophistical uses false premises that resemble truth, intending to nullify truths. It is not genuine argumentation but deception.
The author contrasts it with the rhetorical, which is merely plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس. الأحوال، ويُسمّى القياس المؤلف منه خطابياً إذ يصلح للإيراد في التعليمات والمخاطبات، وقد يكون تارةً مشبهاً باليقين أو بالمشهور المقارب لليقين في الظاهر وليس بالحقيقة كذلك، وهو الجهل المحض، ويسمى القياس المؤلف منه مغالطياً وسوفسطائياً إذ لا يقصد بذلك إلَّا المغالطة والسفسطة، وهو إبطال الحقائق؛ فهذه أربع مراتب لا بد من تمييز البعض منها عن البعض. وأما الخامس الذي يسمى قياساً شعرياً فليس يدخل في غرضنا فإنه لا يذكر لإفادة علم أو ظن، بل المخاطب قد يعلم حقيقته، وإنما يذكر لترغيب أو تنفير أو تسخية أو تبخيل أو ترهيب أو تشجيع وله تأثير في النفس بترديدها على هذه الأحوال وإيجابه انقباضاً وانبساطاً مع معرفة بطلانه. وذلك كنفرة الطبع عن الحلو الأصفر إذا شبه بالعَذِرة حتى يتعذر في الحال تناولها وإن علم كذب قائله، وعليه تعويل صناعة الشعر وبه تشبث أكثر المُتشدّقين من الوعاظ، فإنهم يستعملون في النثر صناعة الشعر، ومثاله أن من يريد أن يحمل غيره على التهوّر، ويصرفه عن الحزم يلقب الحزم بالجبن ويقبّحه ويذمّ صاحبه فيقول: [من الوافر] يَرَى الجبناء أنّ الجُبْنَ حَزْمٌ وَتِلْكَ خَديعةُ النَّفس اللئيمة تعوّد بسط الكف حتى لو أنه دعاها لقبض لم تطعه أنامله تراه إذا ما جئته متهللاً كأنك تعطيه الذي أنت سائله ولو لم يكن في كفه غير روحه لجاد بها، فليتق الله آملُه وهذه الكلمات كلها أحاديث يعلم حقيقة كذبها، ولكنها تؤثر في النفس تأثيراً عجيباً لا ينكر. وإذ ليس يتعلق هذا الجنس بغرضنا فلنهجر الإطناب فيه ولنرجع إلى الأقسام الأربعة، وإذ قد قبّحنا حال الشعر فلا ينبغي أن تظنّ أنّ كلّ شعرٍ باطل، فإن من الشعر لحكمة، وإن من البيان لسحرا. وقد يدرج الحق في وزن الشعر فلا يخرج عن كونه حقاً كقول الشاعر في تهجين البخل: [من الطويل] ومن ينفق الساعات في جمع ماله مخافة فقر فالذي فعل الفقر فهذا كلام حق صادق ومؤثر في النفس، والوزن اللطيف والنظم الخفيف يروجه ويزيد وقعه في النفس، فلا تنظر إلى صورة الشعر ولاحظ المعاني في الأمور كلها لتكون على الصراط المستقيم.
The second consideration: On the matter of the syllogism. The states, and the syllogism composed from it is called rhetorical, since it is suitable for use in instructions and addresses. Sometimes it resembles certainty or the widely accepted that approximates certainty in appearance but is not that in reality; it is pure ignorance, and the syllogism composed from it is called sophistical, since nothing is intended by it except sophistry, and it is the nullification of truths. These are four ranks that must be distinguished from each other. As for the fifth, which is called poetic syllogism, it does not enter into our purpose, for it is not mentioned to produce knowledge or opinion; rather the addressee may know its truth, and it is only mentioned to encourage, discourage, liberalize, make miserly, frighten, or embolden, and it has an effect on the soul by moving it between these states and causing contraction and expansion while knowing its falsehood. For example, the instinctive aversion to yellow sweet if it is compared to excrement, so that it becomes impossible to consume it immediately even if the speaker is known to be lying. Upon this the craft of poetry relies, and most of the pretentious preachers cling to it, for they use the craft of poetry in prose. An example: one who wants to make another reckless and turn him away from caution calls caution cowardice, vilifies it, and condemns its possessor, saying: [al-wafir] 'The cowards see that cowardice is caution, and that is the deception of the vile soul.' He becomes accustomed to opening his hand until if he calls it to close, his fingers do not obey him. You see him when you come to him smiling as if you are giving him what you are asking him for. And if there were nothing in his hand but his soul, he would be generous with it; so let the one who hopes in him fear Allah. All these words are tales whose falsehood is known, but they affect the soul in a wondrous way that cannot be denied. Since this kind does not relate to our purpose, we should abandon elaboration on it and return to the four categories. And since we have disparaged poetry, you should not think that all poetry is false—indeed, some poetry contains wisdom, and indeed, some eloquence is sorcery. Truth may be embedded in poetic meter without ceasing to be truth, as the poet says in condemning miserliness: [al-tawil] 'And whoever spends his days collecting his wealth for fear of poverty—then what he has done is poverty.' This is true and truthful speech that affects the soul, and the pleasant meter and light composition promote it and increase its impact on the soul. So do not look at the form of poetry; observe the meanings in all matters to be on the straight path.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the five types of syllogism according to their matter or premises. The author begins with the four that are within the scope of logic: the demonstrative (برهاني) composed of certain premises, the dialectical (جدلي) composed of widely accepted premises, the rhetorical (خطابي) composed of assumptions, and the sophistical (مغالطي) composed of falsehoods resembling truth. Then he introduces a fifth, the poetic (شعري), which is not used for knowledge or opinion but to influence the soul emotionally. He gives vivid examples: comparing something sweet to excrement to cause aversion, or calling caution 'cowardice' to encourage recklessness. Verses from Zuhayr and al-Mutanabbi illustrate how known falsehoods can still stir the soul. However, not all poetry is false; some conveys truth, like the line condemning miserliness. The author advises focusing on meaning over form. This passage is an expansion of the traditional classification found in matn texts like al-Sullam, where the five arts are enumerated.

Key Terms
القياس البرهاني
القياس الجدلي
القياس الخطابي
القياس المغالطي
القياس الشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) الجدلي (dialectical)
C) الخطابي (rhetorical)
D) المغالطي (sophistical)

Key Takeaway
The four logical syllogisms are ranked by certainty: burhani (certain), jadali (widely accepted), khatabi (plausible), and mughaliti (false but mimicking truth).

The Poetic Syllogism and Its Exclusion from Logic
Author classifies syllogisms into five types by their matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the poetic syllogism and explain why it is excluded from logical inquiry based on its purpose and effect.

Core Idea
The poetic syllogism aims at emotional effect, not knowledge or opinion.

The author introduces a fifth type, the poetic syllogism, but immediately sets it aside because it does not aim at truth. Understanding this distinction is crucial for recognizing when arguments are designed to persuade emotionally rather than provide proof.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find where the author states the purpose of the poetic syllogism: 'لا يذكر لإفادة علم أو ظن' (not mentioned to produce knowledge or opinion).
2
Identify its intended effects
List the emotional states it aims to induce: ترغيب (encouragement), تنفير (discouragement), تسخية (liberality), تبخيل (miserliness), ترهيب (fright), تشجيع (emboldening).
These are not epistemic aims.
3
Analyze the examples
Examine the examples: yellow sweet compared to excrement (aversion), caution called cowardice (recklessness). Note that the hearer knows the falsehood but still feels the effect.
This shows the power of imaginative premises.
4
Understand the author's caution
The author warns that not all poetry is false; some contains truth, like the line condemning miserliness. The form (meter) does not determine truth; meaning does.
This nuance prevents dismissing all poetic rhetoric.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Fourth Certain Premise: Always-Present Middle Term
Introduces the fourth certain premise type with an arithmetic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a premise known by a middle term that is always present in the mind and explain why it can function like a primary premise in syllogisms.

Core Idea
Some mediate premises are so familiar that their middle term is never absent.

The author has classified certain premises into three types: primary propositions, observational propositions, and those derived from continuous testimony (tawatur). Now he introduces a fourth type: propositions that are not self-evident but whose middle term is always present in the mind due to habituation. In this lesson, we will analyse the key example of 'two is one-third of six' and understand why this kind of premise, though technically mediate, is as readily assented to as a primary proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example and its middle term
Read the example: 'two is one-third of six'. Ask: What is the middle term that makes this known? The author says it is that 'everything divided into three equal parts, one part is one-third' and that 'six is divided into three equal parts by twos'. The middle term is the concept of division into three equal parts (al-inqisam ila thalathat aqsam mutasawiya).
2
Contrast with a larger calculation
Now consider the question: 'Is twenty-two one-third of sixty-six?' Here the middle term is the same (division by three), but it is not immediately present. You must consciously divide 66 by 3. Notice the difference: the smaller number (6) and habitual use make the middle term always present.
The author uses this contrast to show that the fourth type depends on the size of numbers and familiarity.
3
Understand why it functions like a primary proposition
Because the middle term is never absent from the mind, the assent is immediate, just like a primary proposition (awwaliyya). The author says it 'runs in the course of primary [propositions]' and is suitable as a premise in syllogisms.
This means even though it is technically mediate, it can be used without additional reflection, making it a certain premise.
4
Generalise the principle
The principle applies to any proposition where the middle term is always present due to habituation, small numbers, or frequent use. In Islamic sciences, for example, the 'illah (effective cause) in qiyas is often so clear and familiar that it is considered manifest (jali).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني: في مادة القياس
الصنف الرابع: القضايا التي عرفت لا بنفسها بل بوسط ولكن لا يعزب عن الذهن أوساطها، بل مهما أحضر جزئي المطلوب حضر التصديق به لحضور الوسط معه كقولنا: الاثنان ثلث الستة، فإن هذا معلوم بوسط وهو أن كل منقسم ثلاثة أقسام متساوية، فأحد الأقسام ثلث والستة تنقسم بالاثنينات ثلاثة أقسام متساوية، فالاثنان إذن ثلث الستة ولكن هذا الوسط لا يعزب عن الذهن لقلة هذا العدد وتعود الإنسان التأميل فيه، حتى لو قيل لك: الاثنان والعشرون هل هي ستة وستين؟ لم تبادر إليه مبادرتك إلى الحكم بأن الاثنين ثلث الستة، بل ربما افتقرت إلى أن تقسم الستة والستين على ثلاثة، فإذا انقسمت وحصل أن كل قسم اثنان وعشرون عرفت أن ذلك ثلثه، وهكذا كلما كثر الحساب؛ فهذا وإن كان معلوماً برأي ثانٍ لا بالرأي الأول ولكنه ليس يحتاج فيه إلى تأمل، فهو جار مجرى الأوليات فيصلح لأن يكون من مواد الأقيسة، بل القضايا التي هي نتائج أقيسة ألفت هي من الأصناف الثلاثة السابقة تصلح أن تكون مواد أقيسة ومقدماتها.
القسم الثاني: المقدمات التي ليست يقينية ولا تصلح للبراهين وهي نوعان: نوع يصلح للظنّيات الفقهية ونوع لا يصلح لذلك أيضاً. المقدمات الصالحة للفقهيات: النوع الأول: وهو الصالح للفقهيات دون اليقينيات وهي ثلاثة أصناف: مشهورات ومقبولات ومظنونات.
الصنف الأول: المشهورات مثل حكمنا بحسن إفشاء السلام وإطعام الطعام وصلة الأرحام، وملازمة الصدق في الكلام، ومراعاة العدل في القضايا والعطف عليهم، والرفق بهم، ومراعاة أحوالهم .
Second inquiry: On the matter of syllogism.
The fourth category: Propositions that are known not by themselves but by a middle term, but their middle terms are never absent from the mind; rather, whenever the two parts of the sought conclusion are brought to mind, assent occurs due to the presence of the middle term with it, as we say: two is one-third of six. For this is known by a middle term, namely that everything divided into three equal parts, one of the parts is one-third, and six is divided by twos into three equal parts, so two is one-third of six. But this middle term is not absent from the mind due to the smallness of this number and one's habituation to contemplating it. So much so that if you were asked: Is twenty-two one-third of sixty-six? you would not rush to judge as you do regarding two being one-third of six. Rather, you might need to divide sixty-six by three, and if it divides and you get that each part is twenty-two, you know that it is its third. Thus, whenever the calculation becomes larger, [this is the case]. So this, although known by a second opinion, not by the first opinion, it does not require reflection; it runs in the course of primary [propositions] and is suitable to be among the matters of syllogisms. Rather, the propositions that are conclusions of syllogisms, if they are composed from the three previous categories, are suitable to be matters of syllogisms and their premises.
The second division: Premises that are not certain and are not suitable for demonstrations. They are two types: a type suitable for fiqh probabilistics and a type not suitable for that as well. The premises suitable for fiqh: The first type, which is suitable for fiqh but not for certainties, is three kinds: well-known (mashhurat), accepted (maqbulat), and probable (maznunat).
The first kind: Well-known [propositions], such as our judgment that disseminating peace, feeding food, maintaining kinship ties, adhering to truthfulness in speech, observing justice in rulings, and being kind to them, and treating them gently, and considering their conditions [are good].
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the 'matter' (material) of syllogisms, i.e., the types of premises. It begins by introducing the fourth category of certain premises: those known by a middle term that is always present in the mind, exemplified by 'two is one-third of six'. The middle term is the concept of division into three equal parts. This kind of premise is so familiar that it seems immediate, though it is technically mediate. The author contrasts it with a larger calculation (22 is 1/3 of 66) which requires explicit division. This fourth type, like primary propositions (awwaliyat), can serve as premises in syllogisms. Moreover, conclusions from the three previous certain categories can themselves become premises.
Then the author moves to non-certain premises, dividing them into two types: those suitable for fiqh (juridical probabilistics) and those not. He lists three kinds suitable for fiqh: mashhurat (well-known opinions, e.g., that it is good to spread peace, feed others, maintain kin, be truthful, be just), maqbulat (accepted from authorities), and maznunat (probable). The passage details the first kind, mashhurat, with examples. These are not certain but are widely accepted and used in legal reasoning.

Key Terms
الوسط (al-wasat): middle term
الأوليات (al-awwaliyyat): primary propositions
الثاني (al-thani): second opinion (mediate knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the proposition 'two is one-third of six' considered a certain premise suitable for syllogisms?
A) It is a primary proposition (awwaliyya)
B) Its middle term (division into three equal parts) is always present in the mind
C) It is based on sense perception
D) It is derived from revelation

Key Takeaway
The fourth certain premise, though mediate, functions like an immediate premise because its middle term is always present due to habituation.

Non-Certain Premises for Fiqh: Mashhurat, Maqbulat, Maznunat
Classifies non-certain premises and details the first kind (mashhurat) with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify non-certain premises into mashhurat, maqbulat, and maznunat, and give examples of mashhurat from Islamic ethics.

Core Idea
Fiqh reasoning often relies on widely accepted opinions (mashhurat), not just certain premises.

After completing the classification of certain premises, the author turns to premises that are not certain and are unsuitable for demonstration (burhan). He divides them into two types: those suitable for fiqh (probabilistic legal reasoning) and those that are not even suitable for that. In this lesson, we will focus on the three kinds of premises that are suitable for fiqh: mashhurat (well-known opinions), maqbulat (accepted from authorities), and maznunat (probable propositions). We will examine the examples of mashhurat provided in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions of non-certain premises
Read the line: 'المقدمات التي ليست يقينية ولا تصلح للبراهين وهي نوعان: نوع يصلح للظنّيات الفقهية ونوع لا يصلح لذلك أيضاً.' So first, non-certain premises are split into those suitable for fiqh probabilistics and those not suitable.
2
List the three kinds suitable for fiqh
The author states: 'وهي ثلاثة أصناف: مشهورات ومقبولات ومظنونات.' These are mashhurat (well-known), maqbulat (accepted from authorities), and maznunat (probable).
3
Focus on mashhurat: definition and examples
Mashhurat are widely accepted opinions about good and bad. The author gives examples: 'حسن إفشاء السلام وإطعام الطعام وصلة الأرحام، وملازمة الصدق في الكلام، ومراعاة العدل في القضايا والعطف عليهم، والرفق بهم، ومراعاة أحوالهم' (the goodness of spreading peace, feeding food, maintaining kinship, being truthful, observing justice, and being kind). These are not certain but commonly held.
These examples come from Islamic ethical teachings and are used in fiqh to derive rulings.
4
Understand why they are used in fiqh
Fiqh rulings often rely on such mashhurat because they represent communal consensus or widely accepted values. While not certain, they provide strong probabilistic support (ghalabat al-zann) for legal decisions.
In usul al-fiqh, mashhurat correspond to principles like istihsan (juristic preference) and maslaha (public interest).

النظر الثاني: في مادة القياس
الصنف الرابع: القضايا التي عرفت لا بنفسها بل بوسط ولكن لا يعزب عن الذهن أوساطها، بل مهما أحضر جزئي المطلوب حضر التصديق به لحضور الوسط معه كقولنا: الاثنان ثلث الستة، فإن هذا معلوم بوسط وهو أن كل منقسم ثلاثة أقسام متساوية، فأحد الأقسام ثلث والستة تنقسم بالاثنينات ثلاثة أقسام متساوية، فالاثنان إذن ثلث الستة ولكن هذا الوسط لا يعزب عن الذهن لقلة هذا العدد وتعود الإنسان التأميل فيه، حتى لو قيل لك: الاثنان والعشرون هل هي ستة وستين؟ لم تبادر إليه مبادرتك إلى الحكم بأن الاثنين ثلث الستة، بل ربما افتقرت إلى أن تقسم الستة والستين على ثلاثة، فإذا انقسمت وحصل أن كل قسم اثنان وعشرون عرفت أن ذلك ثلثه، وهكذا كلما كثر الحساب؛ فهذا وإن كان معلوماً برأي ثانٍ لا بالرأي الأول ولكنه ليس يحتاج فيه إلى تأمل، فهو جار مجرى الأوليات فيصلح لأن يكون من مواد الأقيسة، بل القضايا التي هي نتائج أقيسة ألفت هي من الأصناف الثلاثة السابقة تصلح أن تكون مواد أقيسة ومقدماتها.
القسم الثاني: المقدمات التي ليست يقينية ولا تصلح للبراهين وهي نوعان: نوع يصلح للظنّيات الفقهية ونوع لا يصلح لذلك أيضاً. المقدمات الصالحة للفقهيات: النوع الأول: وهو الصالح للفقهيات دون اليقينيات وهي ثلاثة أصناف: مشهورات ومقبولات ومظنونات.
الصنف الأول: المشهورات مثل حكمنا بحسن إفشاء السلام وإطعام الطعام وصلة الأرحام، وملازمة الصدق في الكلام، ومراعاة العدل في القضايا والعطف عليهم، والرفق بهم، ومراعاة أحوالهم .
Second inquiry: On the matter of syllogism.
The fourth category: Propositions that are known not by themselves but by a middle term, but their middle terms are never absent from the mind; rather, whenever the two parts of the sought conclusion are brought to mind, assent occurs due to the presence of the middle term with it, as we say: two is one-third of six. For this is known by a middle term, namely that everything divided into three equal parts, one of the parts is one-third, and six is divided by twos into three equal parts, so two is one-third of six. But this middle term is not absent from the mind due to the smallness of this number and one's habituation to contemplating it. So much so that if you were asked: Is twenty-two one-third of sixty-six? you would not rush to judge as you do regarding two being one-third of six. Rather, you might need to divide sixty-six by three, and if it divides and you get that each part is twenty-two, you know that it is its third. Thus, whenever the calculation becomes larger, [this is the case]. So this, although known by a second opinion, not by the first opinion, it does not require reflection; it runs in the course of primary [propositions] and is suitable to be among the matters of syllogisms. Rather, the propositions that are conclusions of syllogisms, if they are composed from the three previous categories, are suitable to be matters of syllogisms and their premises.
The second division: Premises that are not certain and are not suitable for demonstrations. They are two types: a type suitable for fiqh probabilistics and a type not suitable for that as well. The premises suitable for fiqh: The first type, which is suitable for fiqh but not for certainties, is three kinds: well-known (mashhurat), accepted (maqbulat), and probable (maznunat).
The first kind: Well-known [propositions], such as our judgment that disseminating peace, feeding food, maintaining kinship ties, adhering to truthfulness in speech, observing justice in rulings, and being kind to them, and treating them gently, and considering their conditions [are good].
