From Quranic Expressions to Universal Premises
Ghazali on extracting universals from Quranic and juristic particulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract a universal premise from a particular Quranic expression by identifying the general meaning intended beyond the specific wording.

Core Idea
A Quranic prohibition expressed with a specific term often targets a broader category.

In this lesson, we examine Ghazali's analysis of Quranic verses and a juristic statement to understand how particular expressions become universal premises. The verse 'Do not say to them 'uff'' uses a minimal annoyance to represent all forms of annoyance. Similarly, the prohibition of consuming wealth unjustly is expressed with 'eating' but covers all forms of destruction. Al-Shafi'i's ruling on snakes and scorpions of Egypt is meant for all deadly creatures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particular expression
Locate the specific word or phrase used in the Shari' source (e.g., 'uff', 'eating,' 'snakes of Egypt').
These are concrete, particular terms.
2
Determine if the expression is representative
Ask: Is this term meant to restrict the ruling only to this specific instance, or is it a common example covering a broader class? Ghazali argues that in these cases, the expression is representative.
3
Extract the universal meaning
Identify the common property that the specific term shares with other similar instances (e.g., annoyance, destruction, lethality). Formulate the universal premise: 'Every annoyance to parents is forbidden,' 'Every destruction of orphans' wealth is forbidden,' 'Every generally lethal creature warrants retaliation.'
The universal premise becomes a kulliyya.
4
Verify by context
Check the broader context of the source: The Quran uses 'uff' to emphasize the least degree of annoyance, implying that greater forms are included by priority. Al-Shafi'i's mention of Egyptian snakes is because they are notorious, not to limit the ruling.
This step ensures the extraction is not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس
الكثير ، وكقوله تعالى: فَلَا تَقُل لَّهُمَا أُفٍّ فعبر عن كلّ ما فيه التبرم به. وكقوله تعالى: وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَلَكُم بَيْنَكُم بِالْبَطِلِ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَأْكُلُونَ أَمْوَالَ الْيَتَمَى ظُلْمًا . والمراد هو الإتلاف الذي هو أعم من الأكل، ولكن عبر بالأكل عنه. وكقول الشافعي : إذا نهشته حيّة أو عقرباء فإن كانت من حيات مصر أو عقارب وجب القصاص. وليس غرضه التخصيص بل كلّ ما يكون قاتلاً الغالب، ولكن ذكر المشهور وعبّر به عن الكلّ فإذا ورد من هذا الجنس لفظ خاص ألغينا خصوصه وأخذنا المعنى الكلي المراد به وقلنا : كلّ تبرم بالوالدين فهو حرام، وكلّ إتلاف لمال اليتامى حرام؛ فيحصل معنا مقدمة كلية . فإن قيل: هو فالمعلوم بواقعة مخصوصة هل هو قضية كلية يفتقر تخصيصها إلى دليل أم جزئية فيفتقر تعميمها إلى دليل وذلك كقوله للأعرابي : اعتق رقبة لما قال : جامعت في نهار رمضان ، وكرجمه ماعزاً لما زنى، فهل ينزل ذلك منزلة قوله : كلّ من زنى فارجموه، وكلّ من جامع أهله في نهار رمضان فليعتق رقبة؟ قلنا: هو كقولك : كلّ موصوف بصفة ماعز إذا زنى فارجموه، وكلّ موصوف بصفة الأعرابي إذا هلك وأهلك بجماع أهله في نهار رمضان فليعتق رقبة . ثم صفة الجماع الذي وصفه السائل والمعتبر من صفات الأعرابي ما عرفه رسول الله ، حتى نزل ترك الاستفصال مع إمكان الإشكال منزلة عموم المقال، حتى إن لم يعرف أنه كان حرّاً أو عبداً كان هذا كالعموم في حق الحرّ والعبد، وإن عرف كونه حرّاً فالعبد ينبغي أن يتكلف إلحاقه بأن يظهر أنه لا يؤثر الرقّ بدفع موجبات العبادات، وإنما نزلنا هذا منزلة العام لأنه قد قال : حكمي في الواحد حكمي في الجماعة. ولو كنا عرفنا من عاداته أنه يخصص كلّ شخص بحكم يخالف الآخر لما أقمنا هذا مقام العام كمن يعلم من أصحاب الظواهر أن المراد بالجزئيات المذكورة في الربويات نفس تلك الجزئيات، ولهذا مزيد تفصيل لا يحتمله هذا الكتاب. وقد بينا عند النظر في صورة القياس أن الحكم الخاص الجزئي إنما يجعل كلياً بستة طرق، وهو بيان أن ما به الافتراق ليس بمؤثر، وأن ما به الاجتماع هو المناسب أو المؤثر ليكون مناطاً، وهو أبلغ في الكشف عن الغرض، وذلك لأن من الجزئيات ما يعلم أن المراد منها كلّي، ومنها ما لا يعلم ذلك كمن لم يعلم من أصحاب الظواهر أن المراد بالجزئيات الست المذكورة في الربويات أمر أعم منها، وعرف كافة النظار أن المراد بالبرّ ليس هو البرّ بل معنى أعم منه، إذ بقي ربا البرّ بعد الطحن إذ صار دقيقاً وفارقه اسم البرّ، فعلم أن المراد به وصف عام كلّي اشترك فيه الدقيق والبرّ، ولكن الكلّي العام قد يعرف بالبديهة من غير تأمل، كمعرفتنا بأن المُحرَّم هو التبرّم العام دون التأفف الخاص، وقد يشك فيه كالبرّ، فإن الدقيق والبرّ يشتركان في كلّيات مثل الطعم والاقتيات والكيل والمالية، وإذا الشك فيه لم يمكن إثباته إلَّا بأحد الطرق الستة التي ذكرناها، والله أعلم.
The second consideration: On the matter of qiyas. [...] And the like, as His saying, exalted: 'Do not say to them 'uff'' – he expressed by it every annoyance. And as His saying: 'Do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly' [...] The intended meaning is destruction, which is more general than eating, but he expressed it by eating. And as the saying of al-Shafi'i: 'If a snake or scorpion bites him, then if it is from the snakes of Egypt or scorpions, retaliation is obligatory.' His purpose is not specification, but rather everything that is generally lethal; but he mentioned the well-known and expressed by it the whole. So when a particular expression of this kind comes, we disregard its particularity and take the universal meaning intended by it, and we say: Every annoyance to parents is forbidden, and every destruction of the wealth of orphans is forbidden; thus we obtain a universal premise. If it is said: Is the known [ruling] from a particular incident a universal proposition whose specification requires evidence, or a particular proposition whose generalization requires evidence? That is like his saying to the Bedouin: 'Free a slave' when he said: 'I had intercourse during Ramadan', and his stoning of Ma'iz when he committed adultery. Is that taken as the position of his saying: 'Every adulterer, stone him', and 'Whoever has intercourse with his family during Ramadan, let him free a slave'? We say: It is like your saying: 'Every one described with the description of Ma'iz, if he commits adultery, stone him', and 'Everyone described with the description of the Bedouin, if he destroys and is destroyed by intercourse with his family during Ramadan, let him free a slave.' Then the description of the intercourse described by the questioner, and the considered among the descriptions of the Bedouin, is what the Messenger of God knew, such that the omission of asking for details despite the possibility of ambiguity was taken as general speech, to the point that if it is not known whether he was free or slave, this is like generality regarding the free and the slave. And if it is known that he was free, then the slave should be attached by effort, by showing that slavery does not affect the repelling of the obligations of worship. We have taken this as general because he said: 'My ruling concerning the individual is my ruling concerning the group.' And if we had known from his custom that he specifies each person with a ruling different from another, we would not have placed this in the position of general, like one who knows from the literalists that the intended meaning of the particular [items] mentioned in riba transactions is those very particulars. And for this there is further detail that this book cannot contain. We have explained in the consideration of the form of qiyas that the particular specific ruling is made universal by six methods, which is the explanation that the distinguishing factor is not effective, and that the common factor is appropriate or effective to be the basis. This is more revealing of the purpose. This is because among particulars, some are known that the intended meaning from them is universal, and among them are those not known like one who does not know from the literalists that the intended meaning of the six particulars mentioned in riba transactions is something more general than them. And all the theorists know that the intended meaning of wheat is not wheat but a more general meaning, since the riba of wheat remains after grinding when it becomes flour and the name wheat departs from it. Thus it is known that the intended meaning is a general universal description that flour and wheat share. But the universal general may be known intuitively without reflection, like our knowledge that the forbidden is general annoyance without specific 'uff' – and it may be doubted, like wheat, for flour and wheat share universals such as taste, nutritiveness, measurement, and monetary value. And when there is doubt about it, it cannot be proven except by one of the six methods we mentioned. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the material of qiyas (syllogism) in logic, specifically on how particular judgments from revelation or prophetic actions become universal premises. Al-Ghazali begins by giving examples from the Quran: the word 'uff' in 'do not say to them uff' is used to express any form of annoyance, not just the word itself. Similarly, the prohibition of consuming wealth unjustly is expressed with 'eating' but actually means any form of destruction. He then cites Imam al-Shafi'i's ruling on snakes and scorpions, which mentions specific locations but intends any generally lethal creature. From these, we discard the particular wording and extract the universal meaning, forming a universal premise like 'Every annoyance to parents is forbidden' or 'Every destruction of orphans' wealth is forbidden.' The objection then arises: When the Prophet gave a specific ruling to a particular person, like ordering the Bedouin who had intercourse in Ramadan to free a slave, or stoning Ma'iz for adultery, does that ruling apply to all similar cases or only to that specific individual? Ghazali’s answer is that we treat it as a universal ruling for everyone who shares the relevant description. The Prophet’s omission of asking for further details despite possible ambiguity (like whether the person was free or slave) indicates generality, because his habit was to treat like cases alike, as he said: 'My ruling on one is my ruling on the group.' However, if we knew his custom was to specify each person, we wouldn't generalize. This echoes the position of the literalists who take the six items mentioned in riba transactions as specific. Ghazali then references his earlier discussion in the form of qiyas about six methods to make a particular ruling universal. These methods involve showing that the differentiating factor is not effective and that the common factor is the suitable cause. Some universals are known intuitively, like that the prohibition of annoyance is general, not just the word 'uff'. In other cases, like the definition of riba in wheat, there is doubt because wheat and flour share multiple common factors (taste, nutritiveness, measurement, monetary value), and we need one of the six methods to determine which is the effective cause. Thus, this passage is a detailed discussion on the inductive generalization of legal rulings within logic.

Key Terms
مادة القياس
قضية كلية
قضية جزئية
التبرم
الاستفصال
الربويات
البرّ
الطرق الستة

C) Only speech acts
D) Only actions that are explicitly mentioned

Key Takeaway
Particular expressions in revelation often serve as representatives for a universal category; the logician extracts the universal premise by recognizing the intended generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Prophetic Precedents as Universal Rulings
Ghazali on generalizing prophetic rulings from particular incidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify the generalization of a prophetic ruling from a specific incident to all similar cases by applying Ghazali's principle of omission of questioning.

Core Idea
The Prophet's omission of clarifying details indicates that his ruling applies universally to all who share the relevant description.

This lesson explores Ghazali's treatment of two prophetic precedents: the Bedouin who had intercourse during Ramadan and was ordered to free a slave, and the stoning of Ma'iz for adultery. Ghazali argues that these rulings are not confined to those individuals but extend to everyone with the same description. The key evidence is the Prophet's omission of asking for further details despite the possibility of ambiguity (e.g., whether the Bedouin was free or slave). This omission signals that the ruling is universal, as the Prophet's practice was to treat like cases alike, as he said: 'My ruling on one is my ruling on the group.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific incident and ruling
For example, the Bedouin's case: he had intercourse in Ramadan and was ordered to free a slave. The ruling is particular to that person.
2
Observe the omission of questioning
The Prophet did not ask whether the Bedouin was free or slave, despite the possibility that the ruling might differ based on that status. This omission (ترك الاستفصال) is significant.
Had the Prophet known of a relevant distinction, he would have inquired.
3
Infer universality
The omission implies that the ruling applies generally to anyone with the same relevant description (e.g., anyone who commits intentional intercourse during Ramadan, regardless of free or slave status).
This is based on the Prophet's known custom: 'حكمي في الواحد حكمي في الجماعة' (my ruling on one is my ruling on the group).
4
Consider exceptions
If we knew the Prophet's custom was to specify rulings for each person, we would not generalize. But the default is generality unless proven otherwise.

النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس
الكثير ، وكقوله تعالى: فَلَا تَقُل لَّهُمَا أُفٍّ فعبر عن كلّ ما فيه التبرم به. وكقوله تعالى: وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَلَكُم بَيْنَكُم بِالْبَطِلِ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَأْكُلُونَ أَمْوَالَ الْيَتَمَى ظُلْمًا . والمراد هو الإتلاف الذي هو أعم من الأكل، ولكن عبر بالأكل عنه. وكقول الشافعي : إذا نهشته حيّة أو عقرباء فإن كانت من حيات مصر أو عقارب وجب القصاص. وليس غرضه التخصيص بل كلّ ما يكون قاتلاً الغالب، ولكن ذكر المشهور وعبّر به عن الكلّ فإذا ورد من هذا الجنس لفظ خاص ألغينا خصوصه وأخذنا المعنى الكلي المراد به وقلنا : كلّ تبرم بالوالدين فهو حرام، وكلّ إتلاف لمال اليتامى حرام؛ فيحصل معنا مقدمة فهل ينزل ذلك منزلة قوله : كلّ من زنى فارجموه، وكلّ من جامع أهله في نهار رمضان فليعتق رقبة؟ قلنا: هو كقولك : كلّ موصوف بصفة ماعز إذا زنى فارجموه، وكلّ موصوف بصفة الأعرابي إذا هلك وأهلك بجماع أهله في نهار رمضان فليعتق رقبة . ثم صفة الجماع الذي وصفه السائل والمعتبر من صفات الأعرابي ما عرفه رسول الله ، حتى نزل ترك الاستفصال مع إمكان الإشكال منزلة عموم المقال، حتى إن لم يعرف أنه كان حرّاً أو عبداً كان هذا كالعموم في حق الحرّ والعبد، وإن عرف كونه حرّاً فالعبد ينبغي أن يتكلف إلحاقه بأن يظهر أنه لا يؤثر الرقّ بدفع موجبات العبادات، وإنما نزلنا هذا منزلة العام لأنه قد قال : حكمي في الواحد حكمي في الجماعة. ولو كنا عرفنا من عاداته أنه يخصص كلّ شخص بحكم يخالف الآخر لما أقمنا هذا مقام العام كمن يعلم من أصحاب الظواهر أن المراد بالجزئيات المذكورة في الربويات نفس تلك الجزئيات، ولهذا مزيد تفصيل لا يحتمله هذا الكتاب. وقد بينا عند النظر في صورة القياس أن الحكم الخاص الجزئي إنما يجعل كلياً بستة طرق، وهو بيان أن ما به الافتراق ليس بمؤثر، وأن ما به الاجتماع هو المناسب أو المؤثر ليكون مناطاً، وهو أبلغ في الكشف عن الغرض، وذلك لأن من الجزئيات ما يعلم أن المراد منها كلّي، ومنها ما لا يعلم ذلك كمن لم يعلم من أصحاب الظواهر أن المراد بالجزئيات الست المذكورة في الربويات أمر أعم منها، وعرف كافة النظار أن المراد بالبرّ ليس هو البرّ بل معنى أعم منه، إذ بقي ربا البرّ بعد الطحن إذ صار دقيقاً وفارقه اسم البرّ، فعلم أن المراد به وصف عام كلّي اشترك فيه الدقيق والبرّ، ولكن الكلّي العام قد يعرف بالبديهة من غير تأمل، كمعرفتنا بأن المُحرَّم هو التبرّم العام دون التأفف الخاص، وقد يشك فيه كالبرّ، فإن الدقيق والبرّ يشتركان في كلّيات مثل الطعم والاقتيات والكيل والمالية، وإذا الشك فيه لم يمكن إثباته إلَّا بأحد الطرق الستة التي ذكرناها، والله أعلم.
The second consideration: On the matter of qiyas. [...] And the like, as His saying, exalted: 'Do not say to them 'uff'' – he expressed by it every annoyance. And as His saying: 'Do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly' [...] The intended meaning is destruction, which is more general than eating, but he expressed it by eating. And as the saying of al-Shafi'i: 'If a snake or scorpion bites him, then if it is from the snakes of Egypt or scorpions, retaliation is obligatory.' His purpose is not specification, but rather everything that is generally lethal; but he mentioned the well-known and expressed by it the whole. So when a particular expression of this kind comes, we disregard its particularity and take the universal meaning intended by it, and we say: Every annoyance to parents is forbidden, and every destruction of the wealth of orphans is forbidden; thus we obtain a universal premise. If it is said: Is the known [ruling] from a particular incident a universal proposition whose specification requires evidence, or a particular proposition whose generalization requires evidence? That is like his saying to the Bedouin: 'Free a slave' when he said: 'I had intercourse during Ramadan', and his stoning of Ma'iz when he committed adultery. Is that taken as the position of his saying: 'Every adulterer, stone him', and 'Whoever has intercourse with his family during Ramadan, let him free a slave'? We say: It is like your saying: 'Every one described with the description of Ma'iz, if he commits adultery, stone him', and 'Everyone described with the description of the Bedouin, if he destroys and is destroyed by intercourse with his family during Ramadan, let him free a slave.' Then the description of the intercourse described by the questioner, and the considered among the descriptions of the Bedouin, is what the Messenger of God knew, such that the omission of asking for details despite the possibility of ambiguity was taken as general speech, to the point that if it is not known whether he was free or slave, this is like generality regarding the free and the slave. And if it is known that he was free, then the slave should be attached by effort, by showing that slavery does not affect the repelling of the obligations of worship. We have taken this as general because he said: 'My ruling concerning the individual is my ruling concerning the group.' And if we had known from his custom that he specifies each person with a ruling different from another, we would not have placed this in the position of general, like one who knows from the literalists that the intended meaning of the particular [items] mentioned in riba transactions is those very particulars. And for this there is further detail that this book cannot contain. We have explained in the consideration of the form of qiyas that the particular specific ruling is made universal by six methods, which is the explanation that the distinguishing factor is not effective, and that the common factor is appropriate or effective to be the basis. This is more revealing of the purpose. This is because among particulars, some are known that the intended meaning from them is universal, and among them are those not known like one who does not know from the literalists that the intended meaning of the six particulars mentioned in riba transactions is something more general than them. And all the theorists know that the intended meaning of wheat is not wheat but a more general meaning, since the riba of wheat remains after grinding when it becomes flour and the name wheat departs from it. Thus it is known that the intended meaning is a general universal description that flour and wheat share. But the universal general may be known intuitively without reflection, like our knowledge that the forbidden is general annoyance without specific 'uff' – and it may be doubted, like wheat, for flour and wheat share universals such as taste, nutritiveness, measurement, and monetary value. And when there is doubt about it, it cannot be proven except by one of the six methods we mentioned. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the material of qiyas (syllogism) in logic, specifically on how particular judgments from revelation or prophetic actions become universal premises. Al-Ghazali begins by giving examples from the Quran: the word 'uff' in 'do not say to them uff' is used to express any form of annoyance, not just the word itself. Similarly, the prohibition of consuming wealth unjustly is expressed with 'eating' but actually means any form of destruction. He then cites Imam al-Shafi'i's ruling on snakes and scorpions, which mentions specific locations but intends any generally lethal creature. From these, we discard the particular wording and extract the universal meaning, forming a universal premise like 'Every annoyance to parents is forbidden' or 'Every destruction of orphans' wealth is forbidden.' The objection then arises: When the Prophet gave a specific ruling to a particular person, like ordering the Bedouin who had intercourse in Ramadan to free a slave, or stoning Ma'iz for adultery, does that ruling apply to all similar cases or only to that specific individual? Ghazali’s answer is that we treat it as a universal ruling for everyone who shares the relevant description. The Prophet’s omission of asking for further details despite possible ambiguity (like whether the person was free or slave) indicates generality, because his habit was to treat like cases alike, as he said: 'My ruling on one is my ruling on the group.' However, if we knew his custom was to specify each person, we wouldn't generalize. This echoes the position of the literalists who take the six items mentioned in riba transactions as specific. Ghazali then references his earlier discussion in the form of qiyas about six methods to make a particular ruling universal. These methods involve showing that the differentiating factor is not effective and that the common factor is the suitable cause. Some universals are known intuitively, like that the prohibition of annoyance is general, not just the word 'uff'. In other cases, like the definition of riba in wheat, there is doubt because wheat and flour share multiple common factors (taste, nutritiveness, measurement, monetary value), and we need one of the six methods to determine which is the effective cause. Thus, this passage is a detailed discussion on the inductive generalization of legal rulings within logic.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Seven Sources of Fallacy in Syllogism
Author enumerates seven sources of error that prevent a syllogism from yielding true conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven sources of fallacy in a syllogism as enumerated by al-Ghazali and identify the first source (invalid figure) due to term ambiguity.

Core Idea
A syllogism fails not only from false premises but from form, clarity, and epistemic ordering.

Al-Ghazali sets the stage for sound reasoning: for a syllogism to yield certain truth, premises must be arranged in a productive figure, terms must be distinct, premises must be true, distinct from the conclusion, and better known than it. Any violation introduces a fallacy. He then lists seven sources of error. Understanding these helps you diagnose weak arguments in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Recall the conditions for a sound syllogism
Al-Ghazali states that syllogistic premises must be (a) in a productive mood of the three figures, (b) the three terms distinct, (c) premises true, (d) premises other than the conclusion, (e) premises more known than the conclusion. Any defect yields fallacy.
These conditions are the backbone for identifying the seven sources.
2
Identify the seven sources enumerated
From the passage, the sources are: (1) not in a valid figure, (2) not in a productive mood, (3) lack of distinctness in terms, (4) lack of distinctness in premises, (5) conclusion included in premises, (6) conclusion prior in knowledge to a premise, and (7) [implied: combination or equivocation? The author says seven, though the list shows six items; the seventh may be implicit in the text's full account].
Memorize these as a checklist for testing any syllogism.
3
Focus on the first source: invalid figure
A syllogism must be in one of the three valid figures (first, second, third). The first figure requires a common term as subject in one premise and predicate in the other. If there is no common term, or the common term is ambiguous, the syllogism is invalid.
The common term is the middle term (ḥadd al-awsaṭ).
4
Recognize the sub‑source: equivocal terms
Error arises when the common term is shared only in wording but differs in meaning (ishtirāk lafẓī). The mind is tricked into thinking a connection exists. Al-Ghazali stresses investigating equivocal terms, especially those resembling univocal terms.
This is a major source of fallacy; he devotes a whole book to it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثالث في المغلطات في القياس وفيه فصول أسباب الغلط الفصل الأول: في حصر مثارات الغلط اعلم أن المقدمات القياسية إذا ترتبت من حيث صورتها على ضرب منتج من الأشكال الثلاثة، وتفصلت منها الحدود الثلاثة أولاً، وهي الأجزاء الأولى، إذ تميزت المقدمتان الأجزاء الثواني، وكانت المقدمات صادقة وغير النتيجة وأعرف منها، كان اللازم منها بالضرورة حقاً لا ريب فيه، والذي لا يحصل منه الحق فإنما لا يحصل لخلل في هذه الجهات التي ذكرناها، إما لخروجه عن الأشكال أو لخروجه عن الضروب المنتجة منها، أو لعدم التمايز في الحدود أو في المقدمات أو لإدراج النتيجة في المقدمات فلا تكون غيرها، أو لأن النتيجة تكون متقدمة على إحدى المقدمات في المعرفة فلا تكون المقدمة أعرف من النتيجة فهذه سبع مثارات؛ فلنشرح كلّ واحدٍ بمثال حتى يَتَيَسَّر الاحتراز (١) عنه فنقول : المثار الأول : أن لا تكون على شكل من الأشكال الثلاثة، بأن لا يكون من الحدود حد مشترك إما موضوع فيهما أو محمول أو موضوع لأحدهما محمول للآخر، فإذا انتفى الاشتراك حقيقة ولفظاً لم يغلط الذهن فيه، فإن ذلك يظهر وإنما يغلط إذا وجد ما هو مشترك لفظاً مع اختلاف المعنى، ولذلك وجب تحقيق القول (٢) الألفاظ المشتركة لا سيّما ما يشتبه منها بالمتواطئة (۲) ، ويعسر فيها درك الفرق، وهو مثار عظيم للأغاليط . وقد ذكرنا تفصيل ذلك على الإيجاز في كتاب «مقدمات القياس»، إلَّا أنا لم نذكر ثمّ إلَّا الألفاظ التي لا يتحد معناها، وقد يكون الاشتراك سببه النظم والترتيب للألفاظ لا نفس الألفاظ، ونحن نذكر من أمثلتها أربعة : الأول : ما ينشأ من مواضع الوقف والابتداء كما ذكرنا من قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا
The Third Consideration: On Fallacies in Syllogism, and it contains chapters. Causes of Fallacy. The First Chapter: On Enumerating the Sources of Fallacy. Know that if syllogistic premises are arranged in terms of their form according to a productive mode of the three figures, and the three terms are separated from them first (they being the primary parts), while the two premises are distinguished as secondary parts, and the premises are true, other than the conclusion, and more known than it, then what necessarily follows from them is truth beyond doubt. That which does not yield truth only fails due to a defect in these mentioned aspects: either because it falls outside the [valid] figures, or outside the productive moods thereof, or due to lack of distinctness in the terms or in the premises, or because the conclusion is included in the premises so it is not other than them, or because the conclusion is prior in knowledge to one of the premises so the premise is not more known than the conclusion. These are seven sources. We shall explain each one with an example to facilitate avoidance of it. So we say: The First Source: That it is not in one of the three figures, meaning that there is no common term among the terms, either as subject in both, or predicate in both, or subject of one and predicate of the other. If the commonality is absent in reality and wording, the mind does not err in it, for that is obvious. Error only occurs when there is a commonality in wording but difference in meaning. Therefore it is necessary to investigate equivocal terms, especially those that resemble univocal terms, and it is difficult to perceive the difference, and it is a great source of fallacies. We have detailed that briefly in the book 'Muqaddimāt al-Qiyās', except that we only mentioned there terms whose meanings are not unified. And commonality may be due to the arrangement and order of words, not the words themselves. We will mention four examples of it: The first: that which arises from positions of pausing and starting, as we mentioned from His saying, the Exalted: 'Except...'
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the seven causes of fallacy in syllogistic reasoning. The author, al-Ghazali, begins the third consideration of his work on logic, focusing on fallacies (al-mughalṭāt). He states that for a syllogism to yield certain truth, its premises must be arranged in a productive mood of the three figures, the three terms must be distinct, the premises must be true, not contain the conclusion, and be more known than the conclusion. Any violation of these conditions introduces a fallacy. He then lists seven 'sources' (mathārāt): (1) not being in a valid figure, (2) not being in a productive mood, (3) lack of distinctness in terms, (4) lack of distinctness in premises, (5) conclusion included in premises, (6) conclusion prior in knowledge, and (7) [the seventh is implied but not explicitly numbered — careful! Actually he lists six explicitly? Let's check: he says 'إما لخروجه عن الأشكال أو لخروجه عن الضروب المنتجة منها، أو لعدم التمايز في الحدود أو في المقدمات أو لإدراج النتيجة في المقدمات فلا تكون غيرها، أو لأن النتيجة تكون متقدمة' — that's six. But he says 'فهذه سبع مثارات'. Possibly the seventh is the combination of two? Or the text might have omitted one. The phrasing 'في المقدمات' could be two separate? Actually 'في الحدود' and 'في المقدمات' are two, so that gives 7: (1) out of figures, (2) out of moods, (3) no distinction in terms, (4) no distinction in premises, (5) conclusion contained in premises, (6) conclusion more known than premise, and (7) maybe something else? The enumeration needs careful reading. But the author states seven. He then proceeds to explain the first source: the syllogism not being in a valid figure due to lack of a common term, especially when the common term is equivocal. He gives the example of the first of four sub-sources: arising from positions of pausing and starting in speech, referencing a Quranic verse that is cut off. This passage is a foundational memorization anchor for understanding logical fallacies.

The Syllogism

Syllogism Not in a Valid Mood
Author identifies fallacious syllogisms not in a valid mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a categorical syllogism in the first figure is invalid because the minor premise is negative or the quantifier is ambiguous.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism needs both true premises and correct form; wrong mood makes even true premises useless.

A syllogism may have true premises but still be invalid if it doesn't fit a productive mood. In this lesson, we examine two examples from Ghazali's Mi'yar al-Ilm: the first involving the quantifier 'a few' and the second involving two negative premises. We will learn how to spot such fallacies and why they fail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed conclusion
In the first example, the conclusion is 'A few people are rational.' In the second, it is 'It is impossible that a human is an animal.'
2
Extract the premises
First example: 'A few people are writers' and 'Every writer is rational.' Second: 'It is impossible that a human is a stone' and 'It is impossible that a stone is an animal.'
Notice the wording of negatives in the second.
3
Check the mood of the first example
The first premise is particular affirmative, the second universal affirmative. Normally this is Darii (first figure), but 'a few' is ambiguous. If 'a few' implies 'only a few and not the many', the syllogism is invalid because the minor premise actually contains two implicit premises.
The author says such confusion arises from clinging to words rather than meanings.
4
Check the mood of the second example
Both premises are negative (impossibility statements). In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative. Here it is negative, so no conclusion follows.
Even though the premises are true, the form is invalid.
5
Apply the first figure rule
The rule: In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative. If it is negative, the syllogism is invalid. Also, if the minor premise is not clearly a categorical proposition (like 'a few people are writers' is problematic), the syllogism fails.
This rule is essential for sound reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار الثاني : ألّا يكون على ضرب منتج من جملة ضروب الأشكال الثلاثة . مثاله قولك : قليل من الناس كاتب وكل كاتب عاقل فقليل من الناس عاقل. وهذه النتيجة صادقة إن لم ترد بإثبات القليل نفي الكثير فإن الكثير إذا كان عاقلاً ففيه القليل، وإن أريد به أن القليل فقط هو كاتب وعاقل اختلط نظم القياس، إذ كان في النظر الثالث : في المغلظات في القياس ، قوله : قليل من الناس كاتب يشتمل على مقدمتين بالقوة : إحداهما بعض الناس كاتب، والأخرى أن ذلك البعض قليل فهما محمولان على البعض. وقد حكم المقدمة الثانية على أحد المحمولين وهو الكاتب دون الثاني فاختلط النظم وكذلك إذا قلت ممتنع أن يكون الإنسان حجراً، وممتنع أن يكون الحجر حيواناً، فممتنع أن يكون الإنسان حيواناً، لأن هذا الضرب ألف من سالبتين غير فيهما اللفظ السلبي، إذ قولك ممتنع أن يكون الإنسان حجراً معناه لا إنسان واحد حجر، بل هذا القدر كاف لنفي النتيجة، فإن صغرى الشكل الأول مهما لم تكن موجبة لم ينتج أصلاً، وإنما تكثر هذه الأغاليط إذا تشبث (1) الذهن بالألفاظ دون أن يحصل المعاني بحقائقها .
The second source: that it is not of a productive mood among the set of moods of the three figures. Example: your saying: ‘A few people are writers, and every writer is rational, so a few people are rational.’ This conclusion is true if you do not intend by affirming the few to negate the many, for the many, if rational, contain the few. But if it is intended that only the few are writers and rational, then the organization of the syllogism is disturbed, since it falls under the third consideration: on grave mistakes in the syllogism. His saying: ‘A few people are writers’ contains two premises virtually: one is ‘Some people are writers,’ and the other is that ‘that some [i.e., the few] are few.’ Thus they are predicates of the ‘some.’ The second premise was applied to one of the predicates, namely ‘writer,’ not to the second, so the organization was disturbed. Similarly, if you say: ‘It is impossible that a human is a stone, and it is impossible that a stone is an animal, so it is impossible that a human is an animal,’ because this type is composed of two negatives where the negative wording is changed. For your saying ‘Impossible that a human is a stone’ means ‘No human is a stone.’ This much is sufficient to negate the conclusion, for the minor premise of the first figure, whenever it is not affirmative, it does not produce at all. These fallacies abound when the mind clings to words without grasping the meanings in their realities.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage discusses the second source of fallacy: when the syllogism is not in a valid mood or figure. The author gives an example: 'A few people are writers; every writer is rational; therefore a few people are rational.' If 'a few' is meant to exclude the many, the syllogism is invalid because the middle term is not properly distributed. The author then analyzes the example, showing it actually reduces to two premises about 'some people' and a problematic application. He also gives a second example with negatives: 'It is impossible that a human is a stone; it is impossible that a stone is an animal; therefore it is impossible that a human is an animal.' This syllogism is invalid because both premises are negative, and the first figure requires the minor premise to be affirmative. The key point: a valid syllogism must have the correct figure and mood; otherwise, even true premises do not guarantee a valid conclusion. The mind often errs by focusing on words rather than logical form.

The Third Source of Fallacy: Synonymous Terms
Author warns against using synonymous words for the three terms of a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a categorical syllogism uses synonymous terms for its terms and explain why it is invalid.

Core Idea
Synonymous terms turn a syllogism into a verbal trick, not a logical proof.

In this lesson, we examine the third type of fallacy in categorical syllogisms: when the three terms are not genuinely distinct because the minor and middle terms are synonyms. The author illustrates with two examples. Understanding this fallacy helps us guard against empty verbal expansions that pretend to be arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
In the first example, the terms are 'human' (minor), 'mortal' (middle), and 'animal' (major). In the second: 'wine' (minor), 'uqar' (middle), and 'intoxicating' (major).
2
Check for synonymy
Determine if any two terms share identical conceptual meanings. Here, 'human' and 'mortal' are synonyms; 'wine' and 'uqar' are synonyms.
3
Verify distinctness condition
A valid categorical syllogism requires all three terms to be conceptually distinct. If the middle term is synonymous with the minor term, the inference collapses into a tautology.
4
Explain why the syllogism fails
The conclusion is not a new logical inference but a restatement of the definitional equivalence of the synonyms. No genuine transfer of knowledge occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار الثالث: ألا تكون الحدود الثلاثة وهي الأجزاء الأولى، متمايزة متكاملة كقولك : كلّ إنسان بشر، وكلّ بشر حيوان، فكلّ إنسان حيوان . وقولك : كلّ خمر عُقار (3) ، وكلّ عقار مُسكر، فكلّ خمر مسكر فإن الحدّ الأوسط هو الحدّ الأصغر بعينه، وإنما تعدّد اللفظ. وهذا من استعمال الألفاظ المترادفة، وهي التي تختلف حروفها وتتساوى حدود معانيها المفهومة، وقد ذكرناها فليحترز منها أيضاً.
The third source: that the three terms—which are the primary parts—are not distinct and complete. Example: your saying: ‘Every human is a mortal, and every mortal is an animal, so every human is an animal.’ And your saying: ‘Every wine is 'uqar, and every 'uqar is intoxicating, so every wine is intoxicating.’ For the middle term is exactly the minor term, only the wording differs. This is from the use of synonymous words, which differ in letters but have equal conceptual boundaries. We have mentioned them, so one should beware of them as well.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage identifies the third source of fallacy: using synonymous terms for the major, middle, and minor terms, so that the middle term is not truly distinct. The author gives two examples: the first uses 'insan' (human), 'bashar' (mortal), and 'hayawan' (animal) where 'bashar' and 'insan' are synonyms. The second uses 'khamr' (wine) and 'uqar' (a synonym for wine) as the minor and middle, and 'musakir' (intoxicating) as the major. In both cases, the middle term is not a true intermediate because it is synonymous with the minor, making the syllogism merely a definitional expansion rather than a logical inference. The author advises caution against such verbal tricks.

Fallacies of Ambiguous Reference in Compound Premises
Author explains fallacies arising from composite premises with unclear reference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify fallacies caused by ambiguous pronoun reference in compound premises and reconstruct the correct logical form to expose the error.

Core Idea
Compound premises can hide ambiguity in pronouns like 'it', making a valid-looking syllogism invalid.

This passage reveals a subtle logical trap: even when premises are true and terms are distinct, ambiguity in compound expressions can break the argument. The author shows how the pronoun 'it' can cause confusion between subject and predicate, leading to a false conclusion. By mastering this lesson, you will learn to detect such fallacies in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous expression
Locate the compound phrase where a pronoun may refer to multiple entities. In the example, the pronoun 'huwa' (it/he) appears in 'فهو كما علمه' (it is as he knows it) and in 'فهو إذن كالكافر' (he is therefore like the disbeliever).
The ambiguity resides in the reference of 'huwa'.
2
Parse the argument's logical structure
Write the premises and conclusion in simple categorical form. Major premise: 'Everything that the Muslim knows, it is as he knows it.' Minor premise: 'The Muslim knows the disbeliever.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, the Muslim is like the disbeliever.'
3
Pinpoint the ambiguity
Determine what 'huwa' refers to in each occurrence. In the major premise, 'huwa' likely refers to the known thing (the object of knowledge). In the conclusion, 'huwa' shifts to refer to the Muslim subject.
This shift is the root of the fallacy.
4
Test the reference consistency
If we keep the reference consistent (e.g., always the known thing), the conclusion becomes 'The known thing is as he knows it' or something nonsensical. The argument only seems valid if we tacitly change the referent.
Equivocation on 'huwa' invalidates the syllogism.
5
Reformulate to remove ambiguity
Replace 'huwa' with an explicit term. For example: 'Everything that the Muslim knows, the known thing is as he knows it; the Muslim knows the disbeliever; therefore, the disbeliever is as he knows it?' That conclusion does not follow, revealing the fallacy.
Explicit reference exposes the logical gap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار الرابع: ألا تكون الأجزاء الثواني وهي المقدمات متفاضلة، وذلك لا يتفق في الألفاظ المفردة البسيطة إذ يظهر فيها محلّ الغلط ولكن يتفق في الألفاظ المركبة، وكم من لفظ مركب يؤدي معنى قوته قوة الواحد أو يمكن أن يدلّ عليه بلفظ واحد، كما تقول : الإنسان يمشي . ثم يمكنك أن تبدل لفظ الموضوع بالحيوان الناطق، ولفظ يمشي بأنه ينتقل بنقل قدميه من موضع إلى آخر حتى يطول اللفظ، ويمكنك أن تعيّن التلبيس فيه . ومن هذا القبيل قولنا : كلّ ما علمه المسلم، فهو كما علمه، والمسلم يعلم الكافر فهو إذن كالكافر، وهذه المقدمات متمايزة الحدود في الوضع ولكن الخلل في الاتساق (3) ، فإنه ترك التصريح بتفصيله، وإلا فقولك : ما علمه المسلم ، موضوع ، وقولك : فهو كما علمه محمول، ولكن تردّد معنى قولك هو، وقد يكون بحيث لا يتميز في الوضع بل يكون فيه جزء يحتمل أن يكون من الموضوع، وأن يكون من المحمول، فإنك تقول : زيد الطويل أبيض؛
The fourth source: that the secondary parts—which are the premises—are not well-proportioned; this does not occur in simple single words because the place of error is apparent there, but it occurs in compound expressions. How many a compound expression conveys a meaning whose strength is that of a single word, or can be indicated by a single word. As you say: ‘Man walks.’ Then you can replace the subject term with ‘rational animal’ and the word ‘walks’ with ‘he moves by moving his feet from place to place’ until the expression becomes long, and you can specify the confusion therein. From this category is our saying: ‘Everything that the Muslim knows, it is as he knows it; and the Muslim knows the disbeliever, so he is therefore like the disbeliever.’ These premises have distinct terms in their wording, but the flaw is in the coherence, because he omitted explicit detail. Otherwise, your saying ‘What the Muslim knows’ is subject, and your saying ‘it is as he knows it’ is predicate, but the meaning of your saying ‘it’ is ambiguous; it may be such that it is not distinct in the wording but contains a part that could be from the subject or from the predicate. For you say: ‘Zayd the tall is white.’
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth source of fallacy: when the premises are composed of complex expressions that obscure the logical relationship. The error occurs not in simple terms but in compound phrases. The author gives an example: 'Everything that a Muslim knows, he is as he knows it; the Muslim knows the disbeliever; therefore he is like the disbeliever.' The flaw is in the word 'it' (huwa) which can refer to the known thing or the knower. The author explains that the terms may be distinct in wording but the ambiguity arises from the incoherence (i'tisāq) of the expression. The solution is to make the reference explicit. The passage illustrates how compound language can hide fallacious reasoning.

الأجزاء الثواني
التركيب
الاتساق

A) The term 'مسلم' is ambiguous.
B) The word 'هو' can refer to the known thing or the knowing Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The coherence of an argument depends on unambiguous reference; compound expressions can hide equivocation.

Fallacies in First-Figure Categorical Syllogisms
Illustrates fallacy of misapplied predication and two negative premises in first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain two fallacies in first-figure categorical syllogisms: misapplied predication and the use of two negative premises.

Core Idea
First-figure categorical syllogisms require an affirmative minor premise; two negatives yield no conclusion.

This passage from al-Miyar illustrates how superficial attention to wording rather than meaning leads to logical errors. The author presents two examples: the statement 'Few people are writers' builds on a misapplied predicate, while the argument 'No human is a stone, no stone is an animal, therefore no human is an animal' violates the first figure's requirement of an affirmative minor premise. Understanding these fallacies equips you to evaluate syllogistic arguments in Islamic sciences critically.

Guided Path
1
Extract the hidden premises in 'Few people are writers'
Identify the two premises implied: 'Some people are writers' and 'That some (the writers) is few'. Notice that the second predicate 'few' is attached to 'writers' instead of to 'some people', creating a confusion of predication.
The phrase 'qalil min al-nas katib' is not a simple categorical proposition but a compressed syllogism.
2
Analyze the stone-human-animal syllogism
Write the syllogism in standard form: Major: No human is a stone. Minor: No stone is an animal. Conclusion: No human is an animal. Check the figure and mood.
3
Determine why the stone-human-animal syllogism is invalid
In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative. Here both premises are negative, so no valid conclusion follows. The conclusion would be false anyway, but the invalidity is formal, not material.
The author emphasizes that the negation wording 'impossible' conceals the negative form.
4
Identify the root cause of both fallacies
Both errors arise from clinging to words without grasping the actual logical relations. In the first, the predicate 'few' is misapplied; in the second, the negative phrasing hides the violation of figure rules.
This reflects the broader principle that logical validity depends on meanings, not verbal formulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قليل من الناس كاتب يشتمل على مقدمتين بالقوة : إحداهما بعض الناس كاتب، والأخرى أن ذلك البعض قليل فهما محمولان على البعض. وقد حكم المقدمة الثانية على أحد المحمولين وهو الكاتب دون الثاني فاختلط النظم وكذلك إذا قلت ممتنع أن يكون الإنسان حجراً، وممتنع أن يكون الحجر حيواناً، فممتنع أن يكون الإنسان حيواناً، لأن هذا الضرب ألف من سالبتين غير فيهما اللفظ السلبي، إذ قولك ممتنع أن يكون الإنسان حجراً معناه لا إنسان واحد حجر، بل هذا القدر كاف لنفي النتيجة، فإن صغرى الشكل الأول مهما لم تكن موجبة لم أصلاً، وإنما تكثر هذه الأغاليط إذا تشبث الذهن بالألفاظ دون أن يحصل ينتج المعاني بحقائقها .
His statement: 'Few people are writers' contains two premises potentially: one is 'Some people are writers', and the other is 'That some is few'. So both are predicated on 'some'. The second premise has judged one of the two predicates, namely 'writer', without the second, so the structure is confused. Similarly, if you say: 'It is impossible for a human to be a stone, and it is impossible for a stone to be an animal, therefore it is impossible for a human to be an animal'. Because this type is composed of two negative propositions with the negative wording changed? Since your saying 'impossible for a human to be a stone' means 'No human is a stone', but this amount is sufficient to negate the conclusion, for the minor premise of the first figure whenever it is not affirmative yields nothing originally. And these fallacies multiply when the mind clings to words without achieving the production of meanings in their realities.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage identifies two examples of fallacious syllogisms. The first example is: 'Few people are writers.' The author explains that this statement contains two implicit premises: 'Some people are writers' and 'That some is few.' The confusion arises because the second premise's predicate 'few' is applied to the first predicate 'writer' instead of to the subject 'some people.' In a proper syllogism, the middle term should be distinct, but here the 'some' is common. The second example is: 'It is impossible for a human to be a stone, and it is impossible for a stone to be an animal, therefore it is impossible for a human to be an animal.' This is a syllogism composed of two negative premises, which is invalid in the first figure because the minor premise must be affirmative. The author emphasizes that such fallacies multiply when the mind clings to words without grasping the true meanings of the concepts. The key lesson is that logical validity depends on the actual relationships of meanings, not on verbal formulations.

شكل أول (shakl awwal, first figure)
محموول (mamlul, predicate)
سلبية (salbiyyah, negative proposition)
موجبة (mujibah, affirmative proposition)

Fallacies in first-figure syllogisms often stem from ignoring the requirement of an affirmative minor premise or from misapplying predicates due to superficial wording.

Fallacy of Synonymous Middle Term in Syllogisms
Defines fallacy where middle term is synonymous with minor or major

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a categorical syllogism commits the fallacy of a synonymous middle term and distinguish it from a valid syllogism.

Core Idea
Synonyms trick you: a valid syllogism needs three distinct terms, not just different words.

In categorical syllogisms, the middle term must be semantically distinct from the minor and major terms. This passage from al-Mi'yar shows that using synonymous words as if they were different terms invalidates the syllogism. You will learn to spot this fallacy and ensure your reasoning uses genuinely distinct concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
List the minor term (subject of conclusion), major term (predicate of conclusion), and middle term (appears in both premises but not conclusion). For the example: 'Every human is bashar, every bashar is animal' — minor=human, major=animal, middle=bashar.
2
Check semantic distinctness
Examine whether the middle term shares the same meaning as the minor or major term, even if the word differs. Here 'bashar' is a synonym of 'human' in Arabic; they denote the same concept.
Focus on meaning, not wording.
3
Evaluate term count
If the middle term is synonymous with the minor or major, then the syllogism has only two distinct semantic terms. A valid categorical syllogism requires three distinct terms.
The fallacy reduces the argument to an invalid form.
4
Test with synonym replacement
Replace the middle term with the synonym of the minor term. If the premises become circular or trivial, the syllogism is invalid. In the example, replacing 'bashar' with 'human' gives 'Every human is human' which is not a genuine premise.
This confirms the lack of a true middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار الثالث: ألا تكون الحدود الثلاثة وهي الأجزاء الأولى، متمايزة متكاملة كقولك : كلّ إنسان بشر، وكلّ بشر حيوان، فكلّ إنسان حيوان . وقولك : كلّ خمر عُقار ، وكلّ عقار مُسكر، فكلّ خمر مسكر فإن الحدّ الأوسط هو الحدّ الأصغر بعينه، وإنما تعدّد اللفظ. وهذا من استعمال الألفاظ المترادفة، وهي التي تختلف حروفها وتتساوى حدود معانيها المفهومة، وقد ذكرناها فليحترز منها أيضاً.
The third cause: that the three terms—which are the primary parts—are not distinct and complete. For example, your saying: 'Every human is a human-bashar, and every bashar is an animal, therefore every human is an animal.' And your saying: 'Every wine is a 'uqar, and every 'uqar is intoxicating, therefore every wine is intoxicating.' For the middle term is exactly the minor term; only the wording differs. And this is from the use of synonymous words, which are those whose letters differ while the boundaries of their understood meanings are equal. We have mentioned them, so one should be cautious of them as well.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the third cause of fallacy in syllogisms: the three terms (subject, predicate, middle) are not genuinely distinct. The author gives two examples. First: 'Every human is bashar, every bashar is an animal, therefore every human is an animal.' Here, 'human' and 'bashar' are synonyms in Arabic, so the middle term 'bashar' is actually the same as the minor term 'human'. Thus, there are only two distinct terms, not three, and the syllogism is invalid because the middle term is not truly separate. Second: 'Every wine is 'uqar (a type of wine), every 'uqar is intoxicating, therefore every wine is intoxicating.' Again, 'wine' and 'uqar' are synonyms. The author explains that this fallacy involves using synonymous words that differ in letters but share the same meaning. He reminds that he has previously discussed such words and warns against them. The lesson teaches that logical terms must be distinct in meaning, not just in wording.

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat): The middle term in a categorical syllogism
الألفاظ المترادفة (al-alfaz al-mutaradifah): Synonymous words; different letters but equal meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The conclusion is false.

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism requires three distinct terms; using synonyms for the middle term reduces it to two terms and invalidates the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following syllogism: 'Every transaction with riba is prohibited. Every sale of dates for dates is a transaction with riba. Therefore, every sale of dates for dates is prohibited.' Check whether the middle term 'transaction with riba' is genuinely distinct from the minor term 'sale of dates for dates' or merely a definitional synonym. Determine if the syllogism commits the fallacy of synonymous terms.
?
Construct a valid syllogism on a fiqh topic where the middle term is a clearly distinct attribute. Then write a version that commits the fallacy by replacing the middle term with a synonym of the minor term. Explain why the second fails.

Identifying Fallacies from Composite Expressions
Explains fallacy from composite terms where parts blur subject/predicate boundary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a composite expression creates ambiguity between subject and predicate in an argument and explain why the fallacy occurs.

Core Idea
Composite expressions can hide scope ambiguities that distort logical inference.

Fallacies often hide in plain sight when terms are not simple. This lesson unpacks the fourth cause: composite expressions where parts blur the boundary between subject and predicate. You will learn to spot such ambiguities using examples from Islamic theology and everyday reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish simple vs composite expressions
Identify whether the expression under analysis is a single term or a compound phrase. Simple terms show error clearly; composites obscure it.
The passage says there is no problem in mufrad (simple) terms, only in murakkab (composite).
2
Trace subject and predicate boundaries
Given a composite expression, split it into possible subject and predicate components. Check if one part could belong to either side.
Example: 'Zayd the tall is white' – 'tall' can be part of subject or predicate depending on wording.
3
Detect scope ambiguity in relative clauses
Look for phrases like 'inasmuch as' or 'from where' that create dual readings. Determine which reading the argument relies on.
The passage uses 'humanity inasmuch as it is humanity is particular or universal' to show two possible interpretations.
4
Apply the test: Does the inference change?
Substitute each possible reading into the argument. If the truth value of premises or conclusion changes, you have found a fallacy of composition.
Example: 'Everything a Muslim knows is as he knows it' – 'it' could refer to the known object or the knower's state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار الرابع: ألا تكون الأجزاء الثواني وهي المقدمات متفاضلة، وذلك لا يتفق في الألفاظ المفردة البسيطة إذ يظهر فيها محلّ الغلط ولكن يتفق في الألفاظ المركبة، وكم من لفظ مركب يؤدي معنى قوته قوة الواحد أو يمكن أن يدلّ عليه بلفظ واحد، كما تقول : الإنسان يمشي . ثم يمكنك أن تبدل لفظ الموضوع بالحيوان الناطق، ولفظ يمشي بأنه ينتقل بنقل قدميه من موضع إلى آخر حتى يطول اللفظ، ويمكنك أن تعيّن التلبيس فيه . ومن هذا القبيل قولنا : كلّ ما علمه المسلم، فهو كما علمه، والمسلم يعلم الكافر فهو إذن كالكافر، وهذه المقدمات متمايزة الحدود في الوضع ولكن الخلل في الاتساق، فإنه ترك التصريح بتفصيله، وإلا فقولك : ما علمه المسلم ،موضوع ، وقولك : فهو كما علمه محمول، ولكن تردّد معنى قولك هو، وقد يكون بحيث لا يتميز في الوضع بل يكون فيه جزء يحتمل أن يكون من الموضوع، وأن يكون من المحمول، فإنك تقول : زيد الطويل أبيض؛ فالمحمول هو الأبيض فقط، والطويل من الموضوع، ويمكن أن يذكر الطويل بصيغة الذي فيرجع إلى زيد بأن تقول : زيد الذي هو طويل أبيض؛ وإن قلت: زيد طويل ، أبيض، صار الطويل جزءاً من المحمول، وإذا لم يذكر الذي يكون بحيث يحتمل أن يراد به الذي وألا يراد كما تقول : الإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية خاصة أو عامة، فيحتمل أن يكون الموضوع الإنسانية المجردة والمحمول الخاصة، ويحتمل أن يكون الموضوع الإنسانية فحسب والمحمول الخاصة من حيث هي إنسانية، إذا لو قلت : الإنسانية خاصة أو عامة لأخبرت عن شيء واحد. فإذا قلت : الإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية خاصة أو عامة أخبرت عن شيئين وكلّ خبر فهو محمول. ولهذا لو قلت الإنسانية ليست من حيث هي إنسانية خاصة ولا عامة، صدق . ولو قلت : الإنسانية ليست خاصة ولا عامة كذب ويفهم الفرق بينهما عند ذكرنا المعنى الكلي في أحكام الوجود، فيتشعب من هذه التركيبات المختلفة أغاليط يعسر حلّها على حذاق النظّار فضلاً عن الظاهريين، ولا تخلص عن مكامن الغلط إلا بتوفيق الله فليستوفق الله تعالى الناظر في هذه العقبات حتى يسلم عن ظلماتها .
The fourth cause: that the secondary parts—which are the premises—are not differentiated. This does not occur in simple singular expressions because the place of error is apparent, but it occurs in composite expressions. How many a composite expression conveys a meaning whose strength is the strength of a single word or can be indicated by a single word. As you say: 'The human walks.' Then you can replace the subject word with 'rational animal', and the word 'walks' with 'he moves by moving his feet from one place to another' until the expression becomes long, and you can specify the obscurity in it. And of this kind is our saying: 'Everything that a Muslim knows, it is as he knows it, and the Muslim knows the disbeliever, so he is then like the disbeliever.' These premises have distinct terms in position, but the defect is in order, for he omitted the clarity of its detail. Otherwise, your saying 'what the Muslim knows' is subject, and your saying 'it is as he knows it' is predicate, but the meaning of your saying 'it is' is ambiguous. It may be such that it is not distinguished in position, but there is in it a part that could be from the subject and could be from the predicate. For you say: 'Zayd the tall is white'; the predicate is only 'white', and 'tall' is from the subject. It is possible to mention 'tall' in the form of 'who' so it refers to Zayd by saying: 'Zayd who is tall is white.' If you say: 'Zayd tall, white', then 'tall' becomes part of the predicate. And if 'who' is not mentioned, it is such that it can be intended as 'who' or not. Just as you say: 'Humanity inasmuch as it is humanity is particular or universal.' It is possible that the subject is abstract humanity and the predicate is 'particular', and it is possible that the subject is humanity alone and the predicate is 'particular inasmuch as it is humanity'. Because if you said 'Humanity is particular or universal', you would inform about one thing. If you said 'Humanity inasmuch as it is humanity is particular or universal', you informed about two things, and every predicate is a predicate. For this reason, if you said 'Humanity is not inasmuch as it is humanity particular nor universal', it is true. If you said 'Humanity is not particular nor universal', it is false. The difference between them is understood when we mention the universal meaning in the judgments of existence. From these various compositions branch out fallacies that are difficult to resolve for the skilled theorists, let alone the superficial ones. And one cannot escape the hiding places of error except with the help of God. So let the one who examines these obstacles seek God's help to be safe from their darkness.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth cause of fallacy: when composite expressions (compound phrases) lead to confusion between subject and predicate. The author notes that simple terms clearly show where the error lies, but composite terms can be ambiguous. For example, 'The human walks' can be expanded with definitions, making the structure misleading. A key example: 'Everything that a Muslim knows, it is as he knows it, and the Muslim knows the disbeliever, so he is like the disbeliever.' This is fallacious because the phrase 'it is' is ambiguous—it can refer to the known object or to the knower. The author then illustrates with 'Zayd the tall is white': here, 'tall' can be part of the subject or predicate depending on wording. Another example: 'Humanity inasmuch as it is humanity is particular or universal' has two possible readings. The author concludes that such compositions produce stubborn fallacies that require divine assistance to avoid. The lesson demonstrates how careful logical analysis demands clear separation of subject and predicate.

The Seventh Source of Error in Qiyas: Circular Premises
Author defines the seventh source of error and illustrates with examples from fiqh and kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism commits the seventh source of error by detecting premises that are no more known than the conclusion, and distinguish circular syllogisms from valid ones.

Core Idea
Premises must be better known than the conclusion; otherwise, the argument is circular or useless.

In this lesson, we examine the seventh source of error in qiyas as outlined by the author: the requirement that the premises be more evident (a'raf) than the conclusion. When premises are equal in knowledge or even less known, the argument becomes either useless or circular. The author illustrates this with examples from correlatives, attribute-substrate reasoning, and a juristic case from the Hanafi school regarding tayammum. By understanding this error, you will be able to spot circular reasoning in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
The seventh source of error is that the premises of a syllogism are not more known than the conclusion. They must be a'raf (more evident or better known). If they are equal, the syllogism is useless; if they are less known, it is circular (qiyas dawri).
2
Identify equal-knowledge examples
The author gives two examples: (1) correlatives (mutadāyifāt) like father and son – proving one by the other is circular because they are known together. (2) Proving that an attribute is knowledge by saying the substrate is knowing – that too requires prior knowledge of the attribute.
Correlatives are known simultaneously; neither can be the cause of knowing the other.
3
Recognize circular syllogisms
When the premise is posterior in knowledge to the conclusion, the reasoning becomes a circular syllogism. The author mentions that such examples are many in intellectual matters.
Circularity can be subtle; the premise may assume the conclusion in disguise.
4
Analyze the Hanafi tayammum example
Extract the syllogism: The Hanafi argues that finding water during prayer implies ability to use water; everyone able must use water; therefore the prayer is invalid. The refutation shows that 'ability' is ambiguous. If sensory ability, it fails when water is owned by another. If legal ability, it depends on the invalidation of prayer – circular.
The middle term 'ability' shifts meaning, causing either refutation or circularity.
5
Apply the refutation logic
Understand why the circularity occurs: legal ability (permission to use water) is not present until the prayer is invalid, so the conclusion is used to define the premise. Thus the syllogism violates the seventh source.
This illustrates how a seemingly valid argument can be flawed due to ambiguous or circular premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار السابع : أن لا تكون المقدمات أعرف من النتيجة، بل تكون إما مساوية لها في المعرفة كالمتضايفات، وذلك مثل من ينازع في كون زيد ابناً لعمرو فيقول : الدليل على أن زيداً ابن لعمرو هو أن عمراً أباً لزيد، وهذا محال لأنهما يعلمان معاً ولا يعلم أحدهما بالآخر، وكذلك من يثبت أن وصفاً من الأوصاف علم بقوله : الدليل عليه أن المحل الذي قام به عالم وهو هوس إذ لا يعلم كون المحل عالما إلا مع العلم بكون الحال في المحل علما. وقد تكون المقدمة متأخرة في المعرفة عن النتيجة فيكون قياسا دوريا، وأمثلته في العقليات كثيرة. وأما في الفقهيات فكأن يقول الحنفي : تبطل صلاة المتيمم إذا وجد الماء في خلالها لأنه قدر على الاستعمال وكل من قدر على استعمال الماء لزمه، ومن يلزمه استعمال الماء فلا يجوز له أن يصلي بالتيمم فيجعل القدرة على الاستعمال حدا أوسط وبطلان الصلاة نتيجة فيقال : إن أردت به القدرة حسا فيبطل بما لو وجده مملوكا للغير، وإن أردت به القدرة شرعا فيقال : ما دامت الصلاة قائمة عليه الأفعال يحرم الكثيرة، فيحرم الاستعمال، فالقدرة شرعا تحصل ببطلان الصلاة، فالبطلان منتج للقدرة والقدرة سابقة عليه سبق العلة على المعلول أعني بالذات لا بالزمان فكيف جعل المتأخر في الرتبة علة لما هو متقدم في الرتبة وهو البطلان؟ فهذه مثارات الغلط وقد حصرناها في سبعة أقسام، ويتشعب كل قسم إلى وجوه كثيرة لا يمكن إحصاؤها.
The seventh source of error: that the premises are not more known than the conclusion, but rather are either equal to it in knowledge, like correlatives. For example, someone who disputes that Zayd is the son of 'Amr says: the proof that Zayd is the son of 'Amr is that 'Amr is the father of Zayd. This is impossible because they are known together and one is not known through the other. Similarly, one who establishes that a certain attribute is knowledge by saying: the proof of that is that the substrate in which it subsists is knowing, which is nonsense, because the substrate is not known to be knowing except with the knowledge that the accident in the substrate is knowledge. And sometimes the premise is posterior in knowledge to the conclusion, resulting in a circular syllogism; its examples in intellectual matters are many. As for juristic matters, it is like the Hanafi saying: the prayer of the one performing tayammum is invalidated if he finds water during it because he is able to use it, and everyone who is able to use water must do so, and whoever must use water is not permitted to pray with tayammum. So he makes ability to use water the middle term and the invalidation of prayer the conclusion. It is said: if you mean by 'ability' sensory ability, then it is refuted by the case where he finds water owned by another; and if you mean by it legal ability, it is said: as long as the prayer continues, many actions are prohibited for him, so using water is prohibited, so legal ability is obtained by the invalidation of the prayer. Thus the invalidation produces the ability, and ability precedes it as the cause precedes the effect – that is, in essence, not in time. So how can the posterior in rank be made the cause of what is prior in rank, namely the invalidation? These are the sources of error, and we have summarized them in seven divisions, each division branching into many aspects that cannot be enumerated.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh source of error in syllogistic reasoning: the requirement that premises be more known (or at least not less known) than the conclusion. The author first states the principle, then illustrates it with two examples from intellectual matters. The first example involves correlatives: if someone tries to prove that Zayd is the son of 'Amr by saying 'Amr is the father of Zayd, this is circular because the two facts are known together and neither can be used to prove the other. The second example is from the problem of proving that an attribute is knowledge by arguing that the substrate (the soul) is knowing; this fails because knowing the substrate is knowing depends on already knowing the attribute is knowledge. Then the author mentions a third case where the premise is even less known than the conclusion, resulting in a circular syllogism (qiyas dawri). The main illustration, however, is a detailed juristic example from the Hanafi school: the issue of whether the prayer of a person performing tayammum is invalidated if he finds water during prayer. The Hanafi constructs a syllogism: the person is able to use water (because he found it), and whoever is able to use water must use it, so he must use it, and thus his prayer becomes invalid. The refutation shows that 'ability to use' can mean either sensory ability (which is refuted by the case where the water is owned by another) or legal ability (which itself depends on the invalidation of the prayer). In the latter case, the reasoning becomes circular: the invalidation is supposed to be the conclusion, but it is used to define the ability which is the middle term. Thus the syllogism commits the error of circularity. The passage concludes by noting that all errors in reasoning reduce to seven sources, each with many branches.

Key Takeaway
The seventh source of error occurs when premises are not more known than the conclusion, leading to either useless equal-knowledge arguments or vicious circularity.

How to Verify a Syllogism and When to Resort to Taqlid
Author discusses how to avoid errors in qiyas, emphasizing adhering to conditions and when to resort to taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the recommended procedure for verifying a categorical syllogism and determine when taqlid is justified.

Core Idea
Certainty in syllogism comes from methodical review; taqlid is intellectual humility, not failure.

After learning the many potential errors in syllogisms, one might feel overwhelmed. The author addresses this anxiety by providing a step-by-step remedy. He emphasizes that errors are localized and can be avoided by carefully observing the terms, premises, and figure. Moreover, he acknowledges that even after diligent checking, some may still lack certainty, in which case taqlid is not only permissible but advisable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms in a given syllogism.
In any categorical syllogism, there are exactly three terms: major, minor, and middle. Practice extracting them from an example like: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.'
2
Check that the terms are grasped as meanings, not mere words.
The author warns against focusing on words only; the concepts must be clear in the mind. For instance, ensure the meaning of 'intoxicant' is properly understood before applying the rule.
3
Verify the premises and the figure.
The two premises must be correctly formed according to the rules of predication and contradiction. Also, confirm that the syllogism belongs to a valid figure (e.g., first figure).
4
Simulate the review process.
Like the arithmetician, go through the reasoning again once or twice. If confidence remains elusive, consider resorting to taqlid. Reflect on what it means to accept authority in matters where personal certainty cannot be reached.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثالث : في المغلظات في القياس. فإن قيل : فهذه مغلطات كثيرة فمن الذي يتخلص منها؟ قلنا : هذه المغلطات كلها لا تجتمع في كل قياس بل يكون مثار الغلط في كل قياس محصورا والاحتياط فيه ممكن وكل من راعى الحدود الثلاثة وحصلها في ذهنه معاني لا ألفاظا، ثم حمل البعض على البعض وجعلها مقدمتين، وراعى توابع الحمل كما ذكرنا في شروط التناقض، وراعى شكل القياس علم قطعا أن النتيجة اللازمة حق لازم فإن لم يثق به فليعاود المقدمات ووجه التصديق وشكل القياس وحدوده مرة أو مرتين كما يصنع الحساب في حسابه الذي يرتبه إذ يعاوده مرة أو مرتين، فإن فعل ذلك ولم تحصل له الثقة والطمأنينة فليهجر النظر وليقنع بالتقليد، فلكل عمل رجال، وكل ميسر لما خلق له.
The third consideration: concerning the serious errors in syllogism. If it is said: These are many errors; who can escape them? We say: These errors do not all come together in every syllogism; rather, the source of error in each syllogism is limited, and caution regarding it is possible. Whoever observes the three terms, and obtains them in his mind as meanings not words, then applies one to the other and makes them two premises, and observes the corollaries of predication as we mentioned in the conditions of contradiction, and observes the figure of the syllogism, will know with certainty that the necessary conclusion is true. If he does not trust that, let him review the premises, the mode of assent, the figure of the syllogism, and its terms once or twice, just as the arithmetician does in his computation which he arranges, reviewing it once or twice. If he does that and still does not obtain confidence and tranquility, let him abandon reasoning and be content with taqlid. For every work has its men, and everything is made easy for what it was created for.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage addresses a practical concern: given the many possible errors in syllogisms, how can one ever be sure of a correct inference? The author responds by first noting that errors do not accumulate in every syllogism; each syllogism has a localized source of potential error. The remedy is to adhere to the three conditions: the three terms (hadud) must be clearly conceived as meanings, not just words; the two premises must be correctly formed; the corollaries of predication (like the rules of contradiction) must be observed; and the figure of the syllogism must be correct. If one does this, certainty follows. If doubt remains, the author recommends reviewing the entire reasoning process multiple times, akin to an arithmetician checking his calculations. If after repeated checks one still lacks confidence, then the honorable course is to abandon independent reasoning and adopt taqlid (following a qualified authority). This is not a failure of intellect but a recognition of individual limits; the author quotes a proverb: 'For every work there are men suited for it.' This passage underscores the epistemological humility required in the Islamic logical tradition and emphasizes that logic is a tool for those who can wield it well.

Key Takeaway
