Syllogism Produces New Knowledge: Refuting the Objection
Author refutes the claim that a syllogism's conclusion is already contained in its premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that syllogisms yield no new knowledge and explain how the author's example of composite/originated body demonstrates that the conclusion is a new piece of knowledge distinct from the premises.

Core Idea
Combining premises in a syllogism reveals new relations, not just old information.

Critics have argued that syllogisms are useless because the conclusion is already 'contained' in the premises. This lesson examines a classical response from Islamic logicians. By analyzing the trivial example of man, animal, and body alongside a substantive theological example (the createdness of the body), the author demonstrates that the conclusion is a third, distinct piece of knowledge that arises only when the premises are actively combined in the mind.

Guided Path
1
State the objection in your own words
The objection claims that if you know the premises, you already know the conclusion, so the syllogism adds no new knowledge. For instance, knowing 'man is an animal' and 'animal is a body' seems to already imply 'man is a body'.
This objection attacks the very utility of syllogistic reasoning.
2
Identify the author's initial reply
The author asserts that knowledge of the conclusion is a third knowledge (ʿilm thālith) additional to the two premises. It is not identical to either premise nor their mere conjunction.
The author uses the term 'third' to emphasize novelty.
3
Examine the trivial example and its function
The author admits that the man-animal-body example appears to make the conclusion obvious, but he clarifies that it is only a 'pure example' (mithāl maḥḍ). It is not meant to showcase the real utility of syllogism, which lies in problematic cases.
Even a trivial example can illustrate the form without capturing the epistemic benefit.
4
Analyze the substantive example
The author provides a genuinely instructive case: one may know that 'every composite is originated' and that 'the body is a composite', yet fail to immediately realize that 'the body is originated'. The relation of origination to body is distinct from the relation of origination to composite. The conclusion emerges only when both premises are brought together with the soul's intention to seek the conclusion.
This example mirrors theological arguments for the temporal origination of the world.
5
Explain why this refutes the objection
The fact that a person can know both premises yet not grasp the conclusion until actively combining them shows that the conclusion is not already known. It is a new relational knowledge produced by the syllogism.
Thus the syllogism is not tautological; it is a genuine tool for discovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قولهم : إن الطريق الذي ذكرتموه في الإنتاج لا ينتفع به، لأن من علم المقدمات على شرطكم فقد عرف النتيجة مع تلك المقدمات، بل في المقدمات عين النتيجة، فإن من عرف أن الإنسان حيوان وأن الحيوان جسم، فيكون قد عرف في جملة ذلك أن الإنسان جسم، فلا يكون العلم بكونه جسماً علماً زائداً مستفاداً من هذه المقدمات . قلنا : العلم بالنتيجة علم ثالث زائد على العلم بالمقدمتين . وأما مثال الإنسان والحيوان فلا نورده إلا للمثال المحض، وإنما ينتفع به فيما يمكن أن يكون مطلوباً مشكلاً، وليس هذا من هذا الجنس بل يمكن أن لا يتبين للإنسان النتيجة، وإن كان كلّ واحدة من المقدمتين بيّنة عنده فقد يعلم الإنسان أن كلّ مؤلف حادث، وهو مع ذلك غافل عن نسبة الحدوث إلى الجسم، وأن الجسم حادث فنسبة الحدوث إلى الجسم غير نسبة الحدوث إلى المؤلف، وغير نسبة المؤلف إلى الجسم بل هو علم حادث يحصل عند حصول المقدمتين وإحضارهما معاً في الذهن مع توجّه النفس نحو طلب النتيجة.
Among them is their statement: 'The method you mentioned for inference is not useful, because whoever knows the premises according to your condition already knows the conclusion along with those premises; indeed, the conclusion is identical to the premises. For whoever knows that man is an animal and that animal is a body, then he has known in that totality that man is a body, so the knowledge of his being a body is not additional knowledge derived from these premises.' We say: Knowledge of the conclusion is a third knowledge additional to the knowledge of the two premises. As for the example of man and animal, we only adduce it as a pure example; rather, it is useful in what can be a problematic object of inquiry, and this is not of that kind. Rather, it is possible that the conclusion may not be evident to a person even if each premise is evident to him. For a person may know that every composite is originated, yet he is unaware of the relation of origination to the body, and that the body is originated. So the relation of origination to the body is different from the relation of origination to the composite, and different from the relation of the composite to the body; rather, it is a new knowledge that occurs upon the occurrence of the two premises and their being brought together in the mind with the soul's orientation towards seeking the conclusion.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage addresses a common skeptical objection against syllogistic inference: if the conclusion is already contained in the premises, then the syllogism yields no new knowledge. The author responds by first insisting that the conclusion is a third piece of knowledge distinct from the premises. He acknowledges that the standard example (man is animal, animal is body, therefore man is body) may seem trivial, but it is only a pure example. The real utility of syllogism lies in cases where the conclusion is not immediately obvious. He then provides a substantive example: one may know that 'every composite is originated' and that 'the body is a composite' without recognizing that 'the body is originated' because the relation of origination to body is distinct from the other relations. The conclusion emerges only when both premises are present together in the mind along with an active intention to seek the conclusion. This shows that syllogism does produce new knowledge.

Key Terms
النتيجة
المقدمات
المطلوب المشكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a syllogism is a new relational knowledge that arises from the combination of premises, not a mere repetition of what is already known.

Causal Analogy: Qiyas al-'Illah
Author defines qiyas al-'illa and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a causal qiyas (qiyas al-'illa) by verifying that the middle term is a cause of the major term.

Core Idea
In causal qiyas, the middle term is the cause that explains why the major term applies.

This passage introduces the first type of qiyas used by jurists and logicians: qiyas al-'illa. In this reasoning, the middle term serves as the cause ('illa) of the major term, providing the reason 'why' the conclusion holds. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the strength of arguments in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the argument
Find the minor term (subject of conclusion), major term (predicate of conclusion), and middle term (linking term in premises).
Example: 'This wood is burnt because fire touched it' – minor: this wood; major: burnt; middle: fire.
2
Check if the middle term is the cause of the major term
Ask: Does the middle term produce or necessitate the major term? If yes, it is a cause.
Fire causes burning, so it is a cause.
3
Classify as qiyas al-'illa if condition holds
If the middle term is a cause, the argument is qiyas al-'illa, also called burhan al-lim (proof of why).
The author says: 'if the middle term is a cause of the major term, the jurists call it qiyas al-'illa'.
4
Verify with a fiqh example
Apply the same test to the fiqh example: 'This substance is impure, therefore prayer is not valid with it.'
In this causal qiyas, impurity is the cause of invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الرابع
في لواحق القياس
وهي فصول متفرقة بمعرفتها تتم معرفة البراهين
فصل في الفرق بين قياس العلة وقياس الدلالة

اعلم أن الحد الأوسط إن كان علة للحدّ الأكبر سمّاه الفقهاء قياس العلة، وسمّاه المنطقيون برهان اللم» أي : ذكر ما يجاب به عن «لم». وإن لم يكن علة سمّاه الفقهاء قياس الدلالة والمنطقيون سموه برهان (الآن)) أي : هو دليل على أن الحدّ الأكبر موجود للأصغر من غير بيان علّته . ومثال قياس العلّة من المحسوسات قولك : هذه الخشبة محترقة لأنها أصابتها النار، وهذا الإنسان شبعان لأنه أكل الآن. وقياس الدلالة عكسه وهو أن يستدلّ بالنتيجة على المنتج فنقول : هذا شبعان فإذا هو قريب العهد بالأكل، وهذه المرأة ذات لبن فهي قريبة العهد بالولادة. ومثاله من الفقه قولك : : هذه عين نجسة فإذن لا تصح الصلاة معها، وقياس الدلالة عكسه وهو أن نقول هذه عين لا تصح الصلاة معها فإذن هي نجسة. وبالجملة الاستدلال بالنتيجة على المنتج يدلّ على وجوده فقط لا على علته، بحدوث العالم على وجود المحدث، وبوجود الكتابة المنظومة على علم الكاتب، ونجعل الكتابة حداً أوسط والعلم حداً أكبر، ونقول : كلّ من كتب منظوماً فهو عالم بالكتابة وهذا قد كتب منظوماً فهو عالم بالكتابة، والكتابة ليست علة للعلم بل العلم أولى بأن نقدر عليته. وكذلك إذا تلازمت نتيجتان بعلة واحدة جاز أن يستدلّ بإحدى النتيجتين على الأخرى فيكون قياس دلالة . ومثاله من قولنا : إن الزنا لا يوجب المحرمية فلا يوجب حرمة النكاح، فإن تحريم النكاح وحل النظر متلازمان وهما نتيجتان للوطء المقتضى لحرمة المصاهرة، فإذا ثبت تلازمهما لعلة واحدة دلّ وجود إحداهما على وجود الأخرى، فإن اختلف شرطهما
The fourth investigation: On the appendices of qiyas. They are scattered sections by which knowledge of demonstrations is completed. Section on the difference between qiyas al-'illa and qiyas al-dalala. Know that the middle term, if it is a cause of the major term, the jurists call it qiyas al-'illa, and the logicians call it 'proof of the why' (burhan al-lim), meaning: mention of what answers 'why'. And if it is not a cause, the jurists call it qiyas al-dalala, and the logicians call it 'proof of the that' (burhan al-anna), meaning: it is evidence that the major term exists for the minor without explaining its cause. An example of qiyas al-'illa from sensible things is your saying: This wood is burnt because fire touched it, and this person is full because he just ate. Qiyas al-dalala is its opposite, namely, inferring from the result to the producer, so we say: This person is full, therefore he is recent in eating; and this woman has milk, therefore she is recent in childbirth. An example from fiqh is your saying: This substance is impure, therefore prayer is not valid with it; and qiyas al-dalala is its opposite, namely we say: This substance is one with which prayer is not valid, therefore it is impure. In summary, inferring the result from the producer indicates only its existence, not its cause, such as inferring the temporal origination of the world to the existence of the Originator, and the existence of orderly writing to the knowledge of the writer. We make writing the middle term and knowledge the major term, and say: Everyone who writes orderly is knowledgeable of writing, and this person has written orderly, therefore he is knowledgeable of writing. Yet writing is not a cause of knowledge; rather knowledge is more deserving of being considered a cause. Similarly, if two results are correlated by a single cause, it is permissible to infer one result from the other, and this is qiyas al-dalala. An example is our statement: Zina does not necessitate prohibition of marriage, therefore it does not necessitate unlawfulness of marriage. For the prohibition of marriage and permissibility of looking are correlated, both being results of intercourse necessitating prohibition of in-law marriage. If their correlation by a single cause is established, the existence of one indicates the existence of the other, unless their conditions differ.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section distinguishing two types of analogical reasoning (qiyas) as used in jurisprudence and logic. The author begins by stating a key principle: if the middle term is a cause ('illa) of the major term, jurists call it 'qiyas al-'illa' (causal analogy) and logicians call it 'burhan al-lim' (demonstration of the reason why). If the middle term is not a cause but merely an indicator, jurists call it 'qiyas al-dalala' (indicative analogy) and logicians call it 'burhan al-anna' (demonstration of the that). The author provides examples from the sensible world: for qiyas al-'illa, inferring fire from burning; for qiyas al-dalala, inferring recent eating from fullness. Fiqh examples are given: from impurity to invalidity of prayer (causal), and from invalidity of prayer to impurity (indicative). The author then clarifies that inferring from effect to cause (qiyas al-dalala) only establishes existence, not causation. An example is provided from writing and knowledge: orderly writing indicates a writer's knowledge, but writing is not the cause of knowledge. Finally, the author discusses cases where two effects share a single cause, allowing inference from one effect to the other, again as qiyas al-dalala. The example from fiqh is that zina does not cause prohibition of marriage, so it does not cause unlawfulness of marriage, using the correlation between prohibition of marriage and permissibility of looking as effects of intercourse. This distinction is crucial for understanding how different types of arguments yield different types of certainty.

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط
علة
برهان اللم
قياس العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, which example best illustrates qiyas al-'illa?
A) 'This woman has milk, therefore she is recent in childbirth'
B) 'This wood is burnt because fire touched it'
C) 'This substance is one with which prayer is not valid, therefore it is impure'
D) 'Writing indicates knowledge of the writer'

Key Takeaway
Causal qiyas (qiyas al-'illa) is defined by the middle term being a cause of the major term, answering 'why' the conclusion holds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following argument: 'This liquid is forbidden because it is an intoxicant.' Identify the middle term, major term, and minor term. Is this qiyas al-'illa? Explain.
?
Check a fiqh ruling: 'Selling dates before they ripen is invalid because the Prophet forbade selling fruit before it becomes good (gharar).' Determine if this is causal or indicative qiyas.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Indicative Analogy: Qiyas al-Dalalah
Author defines qiyas al-dalala and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize indicative qiyas (qiyas al-dalala) by verifying that the middle term indicates the existence of the major term without being its cause.

Core Idea
Indicative qiyas shows that something is true, but not why it is true.

This lesson focuses on the second type of qiyas defined in the passage: qiyas al-dalala, also called burhan al-anna. In this reasoning, the middle term is an indicator (dalil) that the major term exists for the minor, but it is not the cause. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments that prove existence without explaining causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the argument
Find the minor term, major term, and middle term in the argument.
Example: 'This woman has milk, therefore she is recent in childbirth' – minor: this woman; major: recent in childbirth; middle: has milk.
2
Check if the middle term is a cause or an indicator
Ask: Does the middle term produce the major term, or is it a sign? In indicative qiyas, the middle term is a sign, not a cause.
Having milk does not cause recent childbirth; it is an effect that indicates it.
3
Classify as qiyas al-dalala if not a cause
If the middle term is not a cause, the argument is qiyas al-dalala, also called burhan al-anna (proof of that).
The author says: 'if it is not a cause, the jurists call it qiyas al-dalala'.
4
Apply to fiqh example
Check the fiqh example: 'This substance is one with which prayer is not valid, therefore it is impure.'
Prayer's invalidity indicates impurity, but is not the cause of it.

في لواحق القياس

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of qiyas al-dalala mentioned in the passage?
A) 'This wood is burnt because fire touched it'
B) 'This substance is impure, therefore prayer is not valid'
C) 'This woman has milk, therefore she is recent in childbirth'
D) 'The world is temporally originated, therefore it has an Originator'

Key Takeaway
Indicative qiyas (qiyas al-dalala) uses a middle term that is a sign, not a cause, proving only the existence of the major term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the statement: 'This animal is a hyaena because it has the fangs of a predator and eats carrion.' Is this qiyas al-'illa or qiyas al-dalala? Explain.
?
In fiqh, the rule 'Haste is from the devil' is used to infer that rushing in prayer is disliked. Is this causal or indicative? Discuss.
